Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Settlement (Sulh) for an Unknown Claim and Recourse After Partial Istihqaq
Sulh over unknown claim and partial istihqaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a settlement for an unknown claim is valid and determine when a party can recourse after partial istihqaq.

Core Idea
A settlement for an unknown claim is valid; if part of the property is claimed, the settler cannot recourse as long as anything remains in his hand.

This lesson addresses a specific case: a person claims an unknown right in a house and settles for 100. Later, part of the house is claimed from him. Can he recourse against the person he settled with? The text explains that he cannot recourse if any portion remains in his possession, because the original claim may have been valid for that portion. It also highlights that settlement for an unknown is permissible, and its validity does not depend on the validity of the original claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Case
Someone claims an unknown right in a house and settles with the occupant for 100. Later, part of the house is claimed by a third party (istihqaq).
2
Ruling on Recourse
The settler cannot recourse any part of the 100 paid, because his claim may have been in the remaining part. As long as he retains any portion of the house, the settlement is considered valid for that.
3
Exception: Total Claim
If the entire house is claimed, he recourses the full 100, because he paid for something he did not own at all.
4
Principle 1: Sulh over Majhul
Settlement for an unknown amount/right is valid (ja'iz) because it avoids dispute (munaza'ah).
5
Principle 2: Validity of Sulh Independent of Claim
The validity of the settlement does not depend on the validity of the original claim. It is valid even if the claimant cannot prove his claim, as long as the defendant acknowledges or agrees.

Source Text

وفي جامع الفصولين : المشتري إذا زكى شهود المستحق قال أبو يوسف : اسأل عن الشاهدين فإن عدلا رجع المشتري بالثمن على بائعه وإلا يقتصر على المشهود عليه ولا يرجع بثمنه كالإقرار، ثم لو ادعى المشتري استحقاق المبيع على بائعه ليرجع بثمنه فلا بد أن يفسر الاستحقاق ويبين سببه، فلو بينه فأنكر بائعه البيع فبرهن عليه يقبل ورجع بثمنه، وقيل يشترط حضرة المبيع لسماع البينة، وقيل لا وبه أفتى (ظ) بل لو ذكر شبه العبد وصفته وقدر ثمنه كفى شراه عالماً بأنه ليس لبائعه ثم استحق رجع بثمنه للمستحق عليه تحليف المستحق بالله ما باعه ولا وهبه ولا ملکه تصدق به ولا خرج عن بوجه من الوجوه ولو شرى أرضاً فبنى أو زرع أو غرس فاستحق يرجع المشتري بثمنه على بائعه ويسلم بناءه وزرعه وشجره إليه فيرجع بقيمتها مبنياً سلمها إليه، فلو بنى المشتري بناء قيمته عشرة آلاف مثلاً وسكن فيه زماناً . خلق البناء وتغير وانهدم بعضه ثم استحق يرجع على بائعه بقيمة البناء . تسليمه ولا ينظر يوم قائماً لو قال يوم اشترني حتى بثمن نفسه الخ) قال في النهر : فإن أريد بالعبد الذي ظهر أنه فلا إشكال في صحة الكفالة حتى فأنا عبد وقد ضمنت لك الثمن فظهر أنه حر كان للمشتري الرجوع عليه بالثمن ولو كان البائع حاضراً، وإن أريد به الذي يظهر حريته وقد استحق من يد المشتري فسيأتي أنه إنما يطالب بالكفالة بعد العتق ولا كلام في الصحة رجع المشتري على العبد والعبد على البائع بخلاف الرهن ومن ادعى حقاً في دار فصولح على مائة فاستحق بعضها لم يرجع بشيء. إلى ما كان أنفق وإنما يرجع بقيمة ما يمكن نقضه وتسليمه إلى البائع حتى لا يرجع بقيمة جص وطين ولو كان البائع غائباً والمستحق أخذ المشتري بهدم بنائه فقال المشتري غرني بائعي وهو غائب قال أبو حنيفة : لا يلتفت إلى قول المشتري فيؤمر بهدمه وتدفع الدار إلى المستحق، فلو حضر البائع بعد هدمه لا يرجع المشتري على البائع بقيمة بنائه وإنما يرجع عليه لو كان البناء قائماً فسلمه إليه فهدمه وأخذ النقض، وأما لو هدمه فلا شيء على البائع. وهذا بخلاف ما مر في شجر وجص على البائع قيمة الشجر نابتاً في الاستحقاق وللمشتري الرجوع على وكيل البائع بقيمة البناء قائماً وبقيمة الولد للغرور. وإن عرف المشتري إن الدار لغير البائع ولم يدع البائع وكالة فبنى فاستحق لم يكن مغروراً، ولو ادعى المشتري أن البناء له وقال البائع لي فالقول للبائع، وإذا رجع المشتري على بائعه بالثمن وقيمة البناء قال أبو حنيفة : لا يرجع البائع على بائعه إلا بثمنه وعندهما يرجع بهما ا هـ . وتمامه فيه. وفي البزازية من الاستحقاق ظهرت المشتراة حرة ومات البائع لا عن وارث وتركه وبائع البائع قائم نصب الحاكم عن البائع الثاني وصياً فيرجع المشتري عليه وهو يخاصم البائع الأول ا هـ . قوله : ( ومن ادعى حقاً في دار أي مجهولاً فصولح على مائة فاستحق بعضها لا يرجع بشيء) الجواز أن يكون دعواه فيما بقي وإن قل فما دام في يده شيء لم يرجع . قيد باستحقاق بعضها لأنها لو استحق كلها رجع بما دفع للتيقن بأنه أخذ عوضاً عما لا يملكه فيرده. ودل المسألة وضع على شيئين: أحدهما أن الصلح عن المجهول جائز لأنه لا يفضي إلى المنازعة. الثاني أن صحة الصلح لا تتوقف على صحة الدعوى لصحته هنا دونها حتى لو برهن لم يقبل إلا إذا ادعى إقرر المدعى عليه به . قيد بالمجهول لأنه لو ادعى قدراً معلوماً كربعها لم يرجع ما دام في يده ذلك المقدار، وإن بقي أقل منه رجع بحساب ما استحق وفي جامع الفصولين : شراه فبنى فاستحق نصفه ورد المشتري ما بقي على البائع له أن يرجع على بائعه بثمنه وبنصف قيمة البناء لأنه مغرور في النصف، ولو استحق نصفه المعين فلو كان البناء في ذلك النصف رجع بقيمة البناء أيضاً، ولو كان البناء في النصف الذي لم يستحق فله أن . يرد البناء يرجع بشيء من قيمة البناء ولو اشترى نصفه مشاعاً فاستحق نصفه قبل القسمة فالمبيع نصفه الباقي، ولو استحق بعد القسمة فالمبيع نصف الباقي وهو الربع. سئل بعضهم عمن اشترى أرضاً فيها أشجار حتى دخلت بلا ذكر فاستحق الأشجار هل لها حصة من الثمن؟ قال : لا كما في ثوب قن وقنة وبرذعة حمار فإن ما يدخل تبعاً لا حصة له من الثمن إلى آخره وثبت في بعض النسخ كما شرح عليه العيني.
And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If the buyer declares the witnesses of the claimant trustworthy, Abu Yusuf said: Ask about the two witnesses; if they are upright, the buyer recourses for the price against his seller; otherwise, it is limited to the one testified against and he does not recourse for the price, like confession. Then, if the buyer claims the claim of the sold item against his seller to recourse for the price, he must specify the claim and explain its cause. If he explains it and his seller denies the sale and he provides proof against him, it is accepted and he recourses for the price. It is said that presence of the sold item is a condition for hearing the evidence; and it is said no, and this is the fatwa (?) . Rather, if he mentions the description of the slave and his price, it suffices. If he bought it knowing that it is not his seller's, then it is claimed, he recourses for the price. The claimant must be made to swear by Allah that he did not sell it, nor gift it, nor make him own it, nor give it in charity, nor did it leave his ownership in any way. If he bought land and built, or planted, or grew trees, and it is claimed, the buyer recourses for the price against his seller and surrenders his building, plants, and trees to him; then he recourses for their value built and surrendered to him. If the buyer built a building worth, for example, ten thousand, and lived in it for some time, the building wore out, changed, and some of it collapsed, then it is claimed, he recourses against his seller for the value of the building at the time of surrender, and he does not look at the day it was standing. He said: 'If he said on the day he bought me until the price itself...' Al-Nahr said: If what is meant by the slave who appears to be free, there is no issue in the validity of the guarantee such that he said: 'I am a slave and I have guaranteed you the price,' and he appears to be free, the buyer has recourse against him for the price, even if the seller is present. If what is meant is one who appears to be free and has been claimed from the hand of the buyer, then it will come that he is only demanded for the guarantee after emancipation, and there is no speech about validity. The buyer recourses against the slave and the slave against the seller, unlike pledge (rahn). And whoever claims a right in a house and settles for one hundred, then part of it is claimed, he does not recourse for anything. Until what he spent, he only recourses for the value of what can be dismantled and surrendered to the seller, so he does not recourse for the value of plaster and clay. If the seller is absent and the claimant forces the buyer to demolish his building, and the buyer says, 'My seller deceived me,' and he is absent, Abu Hanifa said: Attention is not paid to the buyer's statement; he is ordered to demolish it and the house is given to the claimant. If the seller appears after the demolition, the buyer does not recourse against the seller for the value of his building; he only recourses if the building was standing and he surrendered it to him, then he demolished it and took the debris. As for if he demolished it, there is nothing on the seller. This is contrary to what passed regarding trees and plaster: the seller owes the value of trees growing at the time of the claim, and the buyer has recourse against the seller's agent for the value of the building standing and for the value of the offspring due to deception. If the buyer knew that the house is not for the seller and the seller did not claim agency, then he built and it was claimed, he is not deceived. If the buyer claims that the building is his and the seller says it is mine, the statement is the seller's. When the buyer recourses against his seller for the price and the value of the building, Abu Hanifa said: The seller does not recourse against his seller except for the price, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he recourses for both. End. And its completion is in it. And in al-Bazzaziyyah from the chapter of claim: the purchased (slave girl) appeared to be free and the seller died without an heir, leaving behind property, and his seller exists; the judge appoints a guardian for the second seller, so the buyer recourses against him, and he litigates against the first seller. End. His statement: 'And whoever claims a right in a house, i.e., unknown, and settles for one hundred, then part of it is claimed, he does not recourse for anything' means that his claim may be about what remains, even if a little; as long as something is in his hand, he does not recourse. It is restricted to the claim of part, because if all of it is claimed, he recourses what he paid, because he is certain that he took compensation for what he does not own and returns it. The issue indicates two things: one is that settlement for an unknown is permissible because it does not lead to dispute. The second is that the validity of the settlement does not depend on the validity of the claim, because it is valid here without it, such that if he provides evidence it is not accepted unless the defendant confesses to it. It is restricted to the unknown because if he claims a known amount, like a quarter of it, he does not recourse as long as that amount remains in his hand; if less remains, he recourses according to what is claimed. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: He bought and built, then half is claimed and the buyer returns what remains to the seller; he has the right to recourse against his seller for the price and half the value of the building because he is deceived in half. If the half claimed is specific, and the building was in that half, he also recourses for the value of the building. If the building was in the half that was not claimed, he has the option to return the building and recourses for something from the value of the building? And if he bought half undivided (musha'), then half of it is claimed before division, the sold item is the remaining half; if claimed after division, the sold item is half of the remaining, which is a quarter. Some were asked about a person who bought land with trees that entered without mention, and the trees are claimed, do they have a share of the price? He said: No, like a garment with a cap (qunn) and a saddlecloth (bardha'ah) of a donkey; what enters as a follow-up has no share of the price, etc. And it is confirmed in some copies as explained by al-Ayni.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the discussion on istihqaq, focusing on the buyer's building, planting, and related rights. It begins with a quote from Jami' al-Fusulayn about the buyer's role in certifying witnesses for the claimant. According to Abu Yusuf, if the buyer attests to the witnesses' integrity, he can recourse for the price; otherwise, like confession, he cannot. Then it discusses the necessity for the buyer to specify and explain the claim when suing his seller. There is a dispute about whether the sold item must be present to hear evidence; the fatwa is that it is not required. A description suffices. If the buyer buys knowingly that the item is not his seller's, he still has recourse. The claimant is sworn. When the buyer builds or plants, he recourses for the price and surrenders the improvements, then recourses for their value as handed over. If the building deteriorates before claim, he still recourses for the value at surrender. The passage then discusses a tricky case: if a slave guarantees the price and then appears free, the buyer can recourse against the slave. But if the slave is only claimed, the guarantee is pursued after emancipation. Then it addresses a settlement: if someone claims an unknown right in a house and settles for 100, then part is claimed, he recourses nothing because the claim may be in the remaining part. But if all is claimed, he recourses his payment. The settlement for an unknown is valid, and its validity does not depend on the claim's validity. Then it covers a case where half is claimed and the building is in different parts. Abu Hanifa and the two (Sahibayn) differ on whether the seller's seller recourses only for the price or also for improvements. Abu Hanifa says only price; the two say both. The commentary also discusses a case where the seller is absent, and the buyer is forced to demolish; Abu Hanifa says the buyer's claim of deception is disregarded if the seller is absent. Then a case from al-Bazzaziyyah about a purchased slave girl appearing free, the seller dies heirless, and the judge appoints a guardian for the second seller. Finally, the passage ends with a ruling: if trees are claimed and were included in a land purchase without mention, they have no share of the price, as they are follow-up items.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): Settlement or compromise of a claim, often for an unknown amount.
مجهول (majhul): Unknown; a right or amount not specified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person settles an unknown claim on a house for 100. Later, half of the house is claimed by a third party. How much can the settler recover?
A) 50
B) 100
C) Nothing
D) 25

Key Takeaway
A settlement for an unknown claim is valid; partial claim of the property does not entitle the settler to any recourse as long as some part remains in his possession.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Recourse After Istihqaq: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Seller's Rujūʿ
Intra-Hanafi dispute on chain of recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether the original seller recovers the value of improvements from his own seller, and explain the practical difference (thamarah).

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa limits the seller's recourse to the price; the Sahibayn extend it to include improvements.

When a buyer's purchase is invalidated due to a third party's claim, he may recover the price and the value of any improvements from his seller. But does that seller have the same right against his own seller? The passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq records a clear ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on this very point.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A buyer purchases property (e.g., a house), builds on it, and then a third party proves ownership (istihqaq). The buyer seeks recourse from his seller for the price and the value of the building.
This scenario is the baseline for the dispute.
2
State the buyer's established right
It is settled that the buyer can recover both the price and the value of the building from his immediate seller, regardless of the seller's knowledge. This is due to the concept of ghurur (deception).
The text mentions this right explicitly: 'المشتري على البائع بالثمن وقيمة البناء'.
3
Articulate Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that when the buyer recovers from his seller, that seller (the intermediary) can only recover the price from his own seller. The building value is not passed up the chain.
Text: 'قال أبو حنيفة : لا يرجع البائع على بائعه إلا بثمنه'.
4
Articulate the Sahibayn's position
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the intermediary seller recovers both the price and the building value from his own seller. The chain of recourse is symmetrical.
Text: 'وعندهما يرجع بهما'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشتري على العبد والعبد على البائع بخلاف الرهن ومن ادعى حقاً في دار فصولح على مائة فاستحق بعضها لم يرجع بشيء. إلى ما كان أنفق وإنما يرجع بقيمة ما يمكن نقضه وتسليمه إلى البائع حتى لا يرجع بقيمة جص وطين ولو كان البائع غائباً والمستحق أخذ المشتري بهدم بنائه فقال المشتري غرني بائعي وهو غائب قال أبو حنيفة : لا يلتفت إلى قول المشتري فيؤمر بهدمه وتدفع الدار إلى المستحق، فلو حضر البائع بعد هدمه لا يرجع المشتري على البائع بقيمة بنائه وإنما يرجع عليه لو كان البناء قائماً فسلمه إليه فهدمه وأخذ النقض، وأما لو هدمه فلا شيء على البائع. وهذا بخلاف ما مر في شجر وجص على البائع قيمة الشجر نابتاً في الاستحقاق وللمشتري الرجوع على وكيل البائع بقيمة البناء قائماً وبقيمة الولد للغرور. وإن عرف المشتري إن الدار لغير البائع ولم يدع البائع وكالة فبنى فاستحق لم يكن مغروراً، ولو ادعى المشتري أن البناء له وقال البائع لي فالقول للبائع، وإذا رجع المشتري على بائعه بالثمن وقيمة البناء قال أبو حنيفة : لا يرجع البائع على بائعه إلا بثمنه وعندهما يرجع بهما ا هـ . وتمامه فيه. وفي البزازية من الاستحقاق ظهرت المشتراة حرة ومات البائع لا عن وارث وتركه وبائع البائع قائم نصب الحاكم عن البائع الثاني وصياً فيرجع المشتري عليه وهو يخاصم البائع الأول ا هـ . قوله : ( ومن ادعى حقاً في دار أي مجهولاً فصولح على مائة فاستحق بعضها لا يرجع بشيء) الجواز أن يكون دعواه فيما بقي وإن قل فما دام في يده شيء لم يرجع . قيد باستحقاق بعضها لأنها لو استحق كلها رجع بما دفع للتيقن بأنه أخذ عوضاً عما لا يملكه فيرده. ودل المسألة وضع على شيئين: أحدهما أن الصلح عن المجهول جائز لأنه لا يفضي إلى المنازعة. الثاني أن صحة الصلح لا تتوقف على صحة الدعوى لصحته هنا دونها حتى لو برهن لم يقبل إلا إذا ادعى إقرر المدعى عليه به . قيد بالمجهول لأنه لو ادعى قدراً معلوماً كربعها لم يرجع ما دام في يده ذلك المقدار، وإن بقي أقل منه رجع بحساب ما استحق وفي جامع الفصولين : شراه فبنى فاستحق نصفه ورد المشتري ما بقي على البائع له أن يرجع على بائعه بثمنه وبنصف قيمة البناء لأنه مغرور في النصف، ولو استحق نصفه المعين فلو كان البناء في ذلك النصف رجع بقيمة البناء أيضاً، ولو كان البناء في النصف الذي لم يستحق فله أن . يرد البناء يرجع بشيء من قيمة البناء ولو اشترى نصفه مشاعاً فاستحق نصفه قبل القسمة فالمبيع نصفه الباقي، ولو استحق بعد القسمة فالمبيع نصف الباقي وهو الربع. سئل بعضهم عمن اشترى أرضاً فيها أشجار حتى دخلت بلا ذكر فاستحق الأشجار هل لها حصة من الثمن؟ قال : لا كما في ثوب قن وقنة وبرذعة حمار فإن ما يدخل تبعاً لا حصة له من الثمن إلى آخره وثبت في بعض النسخ كما شرح عليه العيني.
The buyer [has recourse] on the slave and the slave on the seller, unlike the pawn. And whoever claims a right in a house and settles for one hundred, then some of it is claimed, he does not get anything back. Up to what he spent, he only recovers the value of what can be dismantled and delivered to the seller, so he does not recover the value of plaster and mud. If the seller is absent and the claimant compels the buyer to demolish his building, and the buyer says, 'My seller deceived me,' while he is absent, Abu Hanifa said: The buyer's statement is not heeded; he is ordered to demolish it and the house is given to the claimant. If the seller appears after the demolition, the buyer does not recover the value of his building from the seller. He only recovers it if the building was standing and he surrendered it to him, who then demolished it and took the materials. But if he (the buyer) demolished it, nothing is owed by the seller. This is contrary to what passed concerning trees and plaster: the seller owes the value of the trees growing at the time of claim, and the buyer may recover from the seller's agent the value of the building standing and the value of the child due to deception. If the buyer knew that the house belonged to someone other than the seller, and the seller did not claim agency, and he built and then it was claimed, he is not deceived. If the buyer claims that the building is his and the seller says 'it is mine,' the seller's word is taken. When the buyer recovers from his seller the price and the value of the building, Abu Hanifa said: The seller does not recover from his seller except the price, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he recovers both. Etc. Its completion is in it. And in al-Bazzaziyyah from istihqaq: a purchased female slave appeared to be free, and the seller died without an heir and left [something], and the seller's seller is present; the judge appoints a guardian for the second seller, and the buyer recovers from him, and he litigates with the first seller. Etc. His saying: (And whoever claims a right in a house i.e. unknown, then settles for one hundred, then some of it is claimed, he does not get anything back) The permissibility is that his claim remains as to what remains, even if little, so as long as something remains in his hand, he does not get back. He restricted it to 'some of it is claimed' because if all of it is claimed, he gets back what he paid due to certainty that he took compensation for what he does not own, so he returns it. The issue indicates a placement on two things: first that compromise over an unknown is permissible because it does not lead to dispute; second that the validity of the compromise does not depend on the validity of the claim, as it is valid here without it, so even if he presented proof, it is not accepted unless the defendant admitted it. He restricted it to unknown because if he claimed a known amount like a quarter, he does not get back as long as that amount remains in his hand; if less remains, he gets back proportionally to what was claimed. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: He bought it and built, then half was claimed; the buyer returned what remained to the seller; he may recover from his seller the price and half the value of the building because he is deceived as to half. If the specific half was claimed and the building was in that half, he also recovers the value of the building. If the building was in the half that was not claimed, he may return the building; he gets back something of the building's value. If he bought an undivided half, then half was claimed before division, the sold item is the remaining half; if claimed after division, the sold item is half of the remaining, which is a quarter. Someone was asked about a person who bought land containing trees without mentioning them, then the trees were claimed: do they have a share of the price? He said: No, just as in the case of a garment with its nap, a saddle with its girth, and a donkey with its pack-saddle, for what enters as a follow-up has no share of the price. Etc. And it is established in some copies as al-'Ayni commented on it.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the chapter of Istihqaq (claim of ownership) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It presents a series of rulings concerning the scenario where a buyer purchases property (e.g., a house) and builds on it, only to have a third party (al-mustahiqq) prove ownership and take possession. The commentary specifies the buyer's recourse against the seller and the seller's recourse against his own seller. The key intra-Hanafi dispute is highlighted: Abu Hanifa holds that if the buyer recovers the price and value of the building from his seller, that seller does not in turn recover the building's value from his own seller—only the price. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) hold that the intermediary seller recovers both the price and the building's value. The passage also discusses composition (sulh) on a disputed claim: if someone claims a right in a house and settles for a sum, but only part of the house is later proven to be his, the composition remains valid and the claimant does not get back any of the settlement as long as he retains possession of part of the claimed property. This ruling underscores that compromise over an unknown is valid in the Hanafi school, and its validity does not depend on the validity of the underlying claim. The commentary further distinguishes between cases where the buyer knew the property belonged to another (no deception) and where the seller was absent. It also touches on the rule that accessories (like trees in land) have no separate share of the price when sold with the principal item.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): The legal claim by a third party that a sold item actually belongs to them, resulting in the invalidation of the sale.
غرور (ghurur): Deception (in this context, the seller's misrepresentation that he owns the property, leading the buyer to incur expenses).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if the buyer recovers the price and building value from his seller, does that seller recover the building value from his own seller?
A) Yes, he recovers both.
B) No, he recovers only the price.
C) No, he recovers nothing.
D) Yes, but only if the building was standing at the time of claim.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, the chain of recourse after istihqaq is not symmetrical: Abu Hanifa limits the seller's upstream recovery to the price, while the Sahibayn extend it to improvements.

Sulḥ on Disputed Property and the Rule of Accessories
Rulings on composition (sulh) and accessories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a compromise (sulh) on an unknown claim is valid and that partial claim does not disturb the settlement as long as some property remains, and identify when accessories are considered part of the sale without independent price.

Core Idea
A valid sulh on an unknown right survives partial claim; accessories follow the principal and have no independent price.

When a dispute over property is settled by compromise, the settlement is binding even if the claim is unknown. This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that if a claimant settles for a sum and later only part of the property is proven to be his, he does not get back any of the settlement as long as he retains some portion. Additionally, when land with trees is sold without mention, the trees are accessories with no separate share of the price.

Guided Path
1
Understand the ruling on partial claim after compromise
The text says: if someone claims a right in a house and settles for a sum (e.g., 100), then only part of the house is claimed, he does not get anything back as long as he still possesses some of the claimed property.
The reason is that the compromise is over an unknown right; partial proof does not invalidate it.
2
Analyze the principle: validity of sulh over unknown
The passage explains two points: sulh over an unknown is valid because it prevents dispute, and its validity does not depend on the validity of the underlying claim.
Even if the claimant later proves his claim, the settlement stands.
3
Distinguish between principal and accessory
The passage then discusses trees sold with land without mention. If the trees are later claimed by a third party, they have no separate share of the price because they are accessories (tabiʿ).
Analogies: a garment with its nap, a saddle with its girth, a donkey with its pack-saddle.

The buyer [has recourse] on the slave and the slave on the seller, unlike the pawn. And whoever claims a right in a house and settles for one hundred, then some of it is claimed, he does not get anything back. Up to what he spent, he only recovers the value of what can be dismantled and delivered to the seller, so he does not recover the value of plaster and mud. Commentary

It presents a series of rulings concerning the scenario where a buyer purchases property (e.g., a house) and builds on it, only to have a third party (al-mustahiqq) prove ownership and take possession. Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A compromise or settlement of a dispute, often involving payment of consideration.
تبع (tabiʿ): Something that follows or is accessory to the principal item in a sale, such as trees with land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person claims an unknown right in a house, settles for 100, and later only part of the house is proven to be his, does he get any money back?
A) Yes, he gets back a proportional amount.
B) No, because he still possesses some of the property.
C) Yes, but only if the claim was false.
D) No, because the settlement is void.

Key Takeaway
A compromise over an unknown claim is valid and binding even if only part of the claim is later proven; accessories sold with the principal have no independent price share.

Bay' al-Fuduli: The Hanafi Position and the Shafi'i Objection
Commentary on definition, ruling, and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a sale by a non-owner is suspended (mawquf) and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A sale by a non-owner is valid but suspended on ratification—it is not void ab initio.

The sale of another's property without authorization (bay' al-fuduli) raises a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. While the Shafi'is consider such a sale void for lack of authority, the Hanafis see it as valid but suspended, awaiting the owner's ratification. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and legal definition of fuduli, the Hanafi proof from the hadith of 'Urwa al-Bariqi, and the rational arguments that support this position.

Guided Path
1
Define fuduli linguistically and legally
Identify the term's root (fudul = excess) and its technical meaning: a person who transacts in another's property without permission. Understand why it is a relative adjective (nisba) and not derived from the singular.
The Hanafi definition excludes the owner's agent or proxy.
2
State the Hanafi ruling and its conditions
The sale is 'sahih mawquf'—valid but suspended upon ratification. Memorize the four conditions for ratification: existence of the two contracting parties, the subject matter (mabi'), the owner, and (if the price is a specified commodity) its continued existence.
Ratification retroactively validates the contract from its inception.
3
Identify the Shafi'i position and proof
Imam al-Shafi'i holds the sale is void (batil) because it lacks legal authority (wilaya), which is established only by ownership or agency.
For the Shafi'is, the contract is null ab initio and cannot be ratified.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response argues that the contract issues from a qualified person (sane adult) concerning valuable property, and that benefit (maslaha) and implicit permission (idhn dilala) support validity. The hadith of 'Urwa al-Bariqi is cited as direct proof.
The Prophet's supplication for blessing confirms the validity of a fuduli-like sale.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Shafi'i law, the sale is void and cannot be cured; under Hanafi law, the owner can ratify it, making it retrospectively binding. This affects the rights of the buyer and the seller.
The difference is crucial in cases where the owner later wishes to approve the transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيع الفضولي

ومن باع ملك غيره فللمالك أن يفسخه

فصل في بيع الفضولي
ولم تكن ثابتة عند الزيلعي فتركه وهو نسبة إلى الفضولي جمع الفضل أي الزيادة. وفي المغرب : وقد علمت جمعه على ما لا خير فيه حتى قيل :
فضول بلا فضل وسن بلا سنا وطول بلا طول وعرض بلا عرض
ثم قيل لمن يشتغل بما لا يعنيه فضولي وهو في اصطلاح الفقهاء من ليس بوكيل وبفتح الفاء خطأ اهـ . وقيل : الفضولي من يتصرف في حق الغير بلا إذن شرعي كالأجنبي يزوج أو يبيع ولم يرد في النسبة إلى الواحد وإن كان هو القياس لأنه صار بالغلبة كالعلم لهذا المعنى فصار كالأنصاري والأعرابي ؛ كذا في النهاية. وفي فتح القدير: غلب في الاشتغال بما لا يعنيه وما لا ولاية له فيه فقول بعض الجهلة لمن يأمر بالمعروف فضولي يخشى عليه الكفر اهـ قوله : (ومن باع ملك غيره فللمالك أن يفسخه ويجيزه إن بقي العاقدان والمعقود عليه أنه وله وبه لو عرضا) يعني صحيح موقوف على الإجازة بالشرائط الأربعة. وعند الشافعي لا ينعقد لأنه لم يصدر عن ولاية شرعية فيلغو لأنها ثبتت بالملك أو بإذن المالك وقد فقد أولاً انعقاد إلا بالقدرة الشرعية. ولنا أنه تصرف تمليك وقد صدر من أهله العاقل البالغ في محله المال المتقوم فوجب القول بانعاقده إذ لا ضرر فيه مع تخيره بل فيه نفعه حيث يكفي مؤنة طلب المشتري وحقوق العقد فإنها لا ترجع إلى المالك، وفيه نفع العاقد بصون كلامه عن الإلغاء، وفيه نفع المشتري لأنه أقدم عليه طائعاً ولولا النفع لما أقدم فتثبت القدرة الشرعية تحصيلاً لهذه الوجوه، كيف وأن الإذن ثابت دلالة لأن العاقل يأذن في التصرف النافع . واستدل أصحابنا في كتبهم بحديث عروة البارقي أن النبي أعطاه ديناراً ليشتري به أضحية فاشترى شاتين فباع إحداهما بدينار وجاء بالشاة والدينار إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأخبره بذلك فقال عليه السلام: بارك الله لك في صفقتك . ورواه الترمذي عن عروة وحكيم بن خرام كما بينه في النهاية .. وإنما شرط قيام المبيع والمتعاقدين لأن الإجازة تصرف في العقد فلا بد من قيامه وذلك بقيامها كما في الإنشاء، وإن كان الثمن عرضاً أي مما يتعين بالتعيين فلا بد من قيامه أيضاً لكونه مبيعاً، وإنما اشترط قيام المعقود له وهو المالك لأن العقد توقف على

قوله : (ثم رجع) أي أبو يوسف. قوله : (فإنه ينفذ بإجازة الوارث إذا لم يحل له وطؤها) أي بأن كان الوارث ابن الميت وقد وطئها أبوه أو كانت أخته رضاعاً أو ورثها جماعة قد أجازوا كلهم،
Section on the Sale of a Fuduli
245
Whoever sells the property of another, the owner has the option to nullify it or to ratify it, if the two contracting parties and the subject matter still exist, and [if] the right of the owner and [the right] in and by it [are present].
Section on the Sale of a Fuduli
And it was not established according to al-Zayla'i, so he left it. It is a relative adjective from 'fudul' meaning excess. And in al-Mughrib: You have known its plural on what there is no good in, until it was said:
Fudul without fadl, and sunn without sana, and tawl without tawl, and 'ard without 'ard
Then it was said: One who occupies himself with what does not concern him is called a fuduli, and in the terminology of the jurists it is one who is not an agent, and opening the fa is a mistake. It is said: The fuduli is one who acts in the right of another without legal permission, like a stranger who marries or sells. And it is not derived from the singular, though that is what analogy would demand, because it has become predominantly a term of art, like 'Ansari' and 'A'rabi'. Thus in al-Nihayah. And in Fath al-Qadir: It has become predominant for one who occupies himself with what does not concern him and what he has no authority over, so some ignorant people call one who enjoins good a fuduli, fearing disbelief for him. Etc. His saying: (Whoever sells the property of another, the owner has the option to nullify it or to ratify it, if the two contracting parties and the subject matter still exist, and [if] the owner and what is [held] for him and by him [are present]) means it is valid but suspended upon ratification, with four conditions. According to al-Shafi'i, it does not take effect because it did not issue from a legal authority, so it is void, as authority is established by ownership or by the owner's permission, and it is lacking from the outset; a contract is only concluded with legal capacity. Our proof: It is a transfer of ownership and has issued from one qualified to do so—a sane adult—concerning its proper subject, valuable property, so it must be considered concluded, since there is no harm in it with the option, rather there is benefit: it saves the buyer the trouble of seeking [the owner] and the rights of the contract, which do not revert to the owner; it benefits the contractor by preserving his words from being void; it benefits the buyer because he entered into it willingly, and were there no benefit, he would not have entered; thus legal capacity is established to achieve these ends. Moreover, permission is established implicitly because a sane person permits beneficial transactions. Our companions have cited in their books the hadith of 'Urwa al-Bariqi that the Prophet gave him a dinar to buy a sacrificial animal with it, so he bought two sheep, sold one for a dinar, and came with the sheep and the dinar to the Prophet, and informed him of that, and he said: 'May Allah bless you in your transaction.' It was narrated by al-Tirmidhi from 'Urwa and Hakim b. Hizam, as al-Nihaya explains. The condition of the existence of the sold item and the two contracting parties is because ratification is an act concerning the contract, so the contract must exist, and it exists through their existence, just as in initiation. If the price is a commodity specific by specification, it must also exist because it is also sold. The condition of the existence of the owner is because the contract is suspended upon...
It (the section) is the Section on the Sale of a Fuduli. His saying: (Then he returned) meaning Abu Yusuf. His saying: (Then it is executed by the ratification of the heir if sexual intercourse with her is not permissible for him) meaning if the heir is the son of the deceased and his father had intercourse with her, or she was his foster sister, or a group of heirs all ratified.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of bay' al-fuduli—a sale by a non-owner without authorization. The Hanafi school holds that such a sale is not void (batil) but suspended (mawquf) upon the owner's subsequent ratification. The commentary explains the linguistic derivation of 'fuduli' and provides a rebuttal to Imam al-Shafi'i, who considers the sale void because it lacks the capacity (wilaya) of ownership or agency. The Hanafi argument relies on three benefits: for the buyer (saving effort), for the contractor (preventing useless words), and for the seller (who may ratify). Additionally, implicit permission (idhn dilala) is argued because a rational person would permit a beneficial transaction. The proof is the hadith of 'Urwa al-Bariqi, where the Prophet approved his sale of a sheep bought with entrusted money. The passage also lists the four conditions for the validity of ratification: existence of the two contracting parties, the subject matter (mabi'), the owner, and the price if it is a specified commodity. Finally, a note on the heir's ratification is given: if the heir is one to whom the female slave is not permissible (e.g., due to previous intercourse by the deceased or fosterage), his ratification validates the sale.

Key Terms
فضولي (fuduli): A person who transacts in the property of another without authorization (not a proxy, not an owner).
إجازة (ijaza): Ratification by the owner after the fact, which makes the suspended contract binding.
ملك الغير (milk al-ghayr): Property belonging to someone other than the seller.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of a sale made by a fuduli (non-owner)?
A) It is void (batil) from the start.
B) It is valid but suspended (mawquf) on the owner's ratification.
C) It is valid only if the owner explicitly allowed beforehand.
D) It is null and void unless the buyer pays extra.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a fuduli sale as suspended on ratification, based on the hadith of 'Urwa al-Bariqi and the principle of benefit.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Sale by an Unauthorized Person: Ruling and Proofs
Explains the ruling, conditions, and proofs for the validity of a sale by a non-owner subject to ratification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale by an unauthorized person (fuduli), identify the four conditions for valid ratification, and explain the proof from the hadith of 'Urwa al-Bariqi.

Core Idea
A sale by a non-owner is valid but suspended on the owner's ratification—it is not void.

Imagine someone sells your property without your permission. Is the sale automatically void, or can you later accept it? The Hanafi school provides a practical solution: the sale is valid but suspended (mawquf) until you ratify or rescind it. This concept, known as bay' al-fuduli, balances the interests of all parties and is supported by both rational arguments and prophetic practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Focus on the act: selling another's property without prior permission. State the Hanafi ruling: the contract is valid (mun'akid) but suspended (mawquf) on the owner's ratification. The owner has the right to rescind (faskh) or ratify (ijaza).
Note: This is the mufta bihi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith of 'Urwa al-Bariqi. The Prophet (peace be upon him) gave him a dinar to buy a sacrifice; he bought two sheep and sold one, then informed the Prophet, who blessed the transaction. This taqrir (tacit approval) indicates permissibility.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted for practice.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's approval of a sale without his explicit prior permission shows that such a transaction is valid. The absence of objection and the blessing (baraka) demonstrate permissibility. This establishes that a non-owner's sale can be effective subject to subsequent ratification.
The dalala is zanni but sufficient for establishing the ruling.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (Shafi'i opposition)
The Shafi'i school holds that the contract is void (batil) from the outset because it lacks legal authority (wilaya) from ownership or permission. The Hanafi response: the contract is from a qualified person (adult, sane) concerning a proper subject, and implicit permission (dalalat al-i'dhan) exists for beneficial acts.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in Hanafi, ratification retroactively validates the sale; in Shafi'i, a new contract is needed.
5
Analyze the conditions for ratification
Ratification (ijaza) requires four conditions: (1) the sold item exists, (2) the two contracting parties (buyer and seller) are alive, (3) the owner is alive, (4) if the price is a specific commodity, it also exists. An intra-Hanafi dispute exists: if existence of the sold item is unknown, Abu Yusuf presumes it exists (valid), while Muhammad requires certainty.
The condition that the owner is alive is specific; his heir cannot ratify because the suspension is tied to his will.

Source Text

Chapter on the Sale by an Unauthorized Person (Fuduli). And whoever sells the property of another, the owner has the right to rescind it or ratify it if the two contracting parties and the object of the contract remain. (His statement: 'And whoever sells the property of another, the owner has the right to rescind it or ratify it if the two contracting parties and the object of the contract remain, and the owner remains and the price remains' meaning it is valid but suspended on ratification with four conditions. According to al-Shafi'i, it does not take effect because it did not issue from legal authority and is thus void; legal authority is established by ownership or by the owner's permission, and both are initially absent. For us (Hanafis), it is a proprietary disposition issued from a qualified person (sane adult) concerning its proper subject (valuable property), so it must be considered effective; there is no harm in it since he has the choice (of ratifying), rather there is benefit: it saves the purchaser the trouble of seeking and the contractual rights that do not revert to the owner; it benefits the contracting party by preserving his words from nullification; it benefits the purchaser because he entered willingly, else he would not have entered. Thus, the legal capacity (to conclude) is established to attain these benefits. Moreover, permission is implicitly established because a sane person permits beneficial dispositions. Our companions have cited in their books the hadith of 'Urwa al-Bariqi: the Prophet (peace be upon him) gave him a dinar to buy a sacrifice; he bought two sheep and sold one for a dinar and came to the Prophet with the sheep and the dinar, informing him, and he said: 'May Allah bless you in your transaction.' It was narrated by al-Tirmidhi from 'Urwa and Hakim ibn Hizam as explained in al-Nihaya. ... The condition that the sold object and the contracting parties remain is because ratification is a disposition on the contract, so its existence is necessary; that is by their existence as in the original creation. If the price is a specific commodity (mu'ayyan), its existence is also necessary since it is like sold goods. The condition of the owner's existence is because the contract is suspended on his ratification, so it cannot be effective by another's ratification. If the owner dies, it is not effective by the heir's ratification, unlike a suspended division (qisma mawqufa) which becomes effective by the heir's ratification according to the second view; so in al-Bazzaziyya. If the state of the sold item at the time of ratification (whether it still exists or not) is unknown, the sale is valid according to Abu Yusuf, but not according to Muhammad, because the default is its continued existence. Then he said: 'and he did not retract' and said 'it is valid as long as its existence is not known' in the view of both because doubt arises about a condition of ratification, so it is not established with doubt. It is restricted to sale because a suspended marriage contract is not invalidated by the death of the contracting party. If a slave-girl marries without her master's permission and then the master dies, it is effective by the heir's ratification if her marriage is not permissible for him. And if the owner ratifies the sale and the price is cash, it becomes a trust (amana) in the hands of the unauthorized seller like an agent, because subsequent ratification is like prior agency. If the owner does not ratify and the price perishes in the hands of the unauthorized seller, the jurists differed on whether the buyer can seek recourse for its equivalent; the most correct is that if the buyer knew he was an unauthorized seller at the time of payment, he has no recourse; otherwise he does. So it is mentioned in al-Qunyah. And the commentator explicitly stated that it is a trust in his hand, so there is no liability if it perishes, whether before or after ratification. If the price is a commodity, it becomes the property of the unauthorized seller. The owner's ratification is a ratification of the cash (price) not of the contract, because when the exchange is specific, it is a purchase in some sense, and purchase is not suspended but effective directly for the doer if it is valid; thus it becomes his property, and by the owner's ratification it does not transfer to him, rather its effect is on the cash not the contract. Then the unauthorized seller must give the like of the sold item if it is fungible (mithli), otherwise its value if it is non-fungible (qimi), because when the substitute became his, he becomes a purchaser for himself with another's property, borrowing it implicitly through the purchase, so he must return it. This is like if he paid his debt with another's property, and borrowing non-fungible things is valid implicitly, not explicitly. Do you not see that if a man marries a woman with a slave belonging to another, it is valid and he must pay its value. And the existence of the sold item is not required in one case of the unauthorized sale mentioned in the chapter on found property (luqata): the finder if he sells the found item without the qadi's order, then its owner comes after the item has perished, he may hold the seller liable, and then the sale becomes effective from the seller's side in the apparent narration, and the majority of scholars adopted it. Similarly, they said about the finder if he gives it in charity and the item perishes, then the owner ratifies after perishing, it is valid. Summary: The restriction to the owner in his statement 'the owner has the right to rescind or ratify' is because the unauthorized seller has only the right to rescind, even if he ratifies it does not take effect because the suspended contract is already void; his right is to protect himself from liabilities. So if some of them do not ratify ... but if he inherits from her marriage forbidden to him, the suspended marriage becomes invalid as mentioned in the chapter on slave's marriage because a permissible relation occurs over a suspended contract. And the commentator stated that it is a trust in his hand. In Manh al-Ghaffar: but what was corrected in al-Qunyah was relied upon by our teacher's teacher 'Abd al-Barr in his commentary on al-Nazm al-Wahbani. His statement: 'And the owner's ratification is a ratification of the cash not the contract' means ratification that the seller pays what he sold as a price for what he owned by the contract, not a ratification of the contract, because the contract is binding on the unauthorized seller. Guidance. His statement: 'and the existence of the sold item is not required in the case' said al-Ramli: this case is exceptional.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on sale by an unauthorized person (fuduli). The author explains the ruling: If someone sells another's property without permission, the sale is valid but suspended (mawquf) on the owner's ratification. The owner has the option to either rescind (faskh) or ratify (ijaza). This is the Hanafi position. The Shafi'i school holds that such a contract is void (batil) from the start because it lacks legal authority (wilaya). The Hanafis argue that the contract is a valid disposition because it comes from a qualified person (adult, sane) concerning a proper subject (valuable property), and it brings benefits to all parties. They also provide a hadith: The Prophet (peace be upon him) approved of 'Urwa al-Bariqi's transaction where he sold one of two sheep he bought with the Prophet's dinar, indicating that such a sale is valid even without prior explicit permission. The conditions for valid ratification are four: (1) the sold item must still exist, (2) the two contracting parties (buyer and seller) must be alive, (3) the owner must be alive, and (4) if the price is a specific commodity (ayn), it must also exist. If the owner dies, his heir cannot ratify because the contract was suspended on the owner's specific permission. There is an exception in the case of a suspended division (qisma). There is also an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the status of the sold item is unknown at the time of ratification, Abu Yusuf says the sale is valid (default is existence), while Muhammad says it is not valid because doubt about a condition of validity prevents it. The author also discusses what happens after ratification: if the price was cash, it becomes a trust (amana) in the hands of the unauthorized seller (now like an agent). If the price was a specific commodity, the unauthorized seller becomes its owner, and the owner's ratification is only ratification of the price, not the contract. In that case, the unauthorized seller must provide an equivalent (mithl) of the sold item if fungible, or its value if non-fungible. Finally, there is an exceptional case from the chapter on found property (luqata): if a finder sells a found item without court order and the item perishes, the owner can hold the seller liable, and the sale becomes effective from the seller's side (so the buyer gets the item? Actually, the text says the sale becomes effective from the seller's side, meaning the seller is considered the owner? But the ruling is specific). This shows that the requirement of the item's existence is not absolute.

Key Terms
فضولي (fudūlī): A person who acts in another's right without proper legal authority (wilaya) or permission, e.g., selling another's property without being an agent.
عقد موقوف (ʿaqd mawqūf): A contract that is valid but not effective yet; its effect is suspended on a condition (like ratification) but becomes binding when the condition is fulfilled.
الإجازة (al-ijāza): Ratification or approval by the owner after the fact, which makes the suspended contract effective from its inception.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition regarding the sold item for the owner's ratification to be valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) The sold item must still exist at the time of ratification.
B) The sold item must have been in the owner's possession at the time of sale.
C) The sold item must be fungible (mithli).
D) The sold item must be measured by weight or capacity.

Key Takeaway
A sale by an unauthorized person (fuduli) is valid but suspended on the owner's ratification; it is not void, benefiting all parties and supported by the hadith of 'Urwa al-Bariqi.

Death of Owner Before Ratification and Dispute on Existence
Rulings on ratification after owner's death and dispute on required existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on ratification by an heir after the owner's death in a fuduli sale, and explain the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding the requirement of knowledge of the item's existence at ratification.

Core Idea
Ratification of a fuduli sale dies with the owner; existence of the item must be known for ratification according to Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf presumes existence.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the limits of ratification (ijazah) in a fuduli contract. It addresses two critical scenarios: the death of the owner before ratification, and the requirement that the sold item still exist. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad on the second point, grounded in their different treatment of doubt (shakk) in contractual conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage states that ratification by someone other than the owner is not effective. If the owner dies, the heir cannot ratify the sale.
This is the default ruling for fuduli sales.
2
Note the exception for division
Unlike sale, a suspended division (qisma mawqufa) can be ratified by the heir according to Abu Yusuf. This shows that the rule is not absolute.
The exception is based on the nature of division.
3
Locate the dispute on existence
The passage presents a second issue: if the existence of the sold item at ratification is unknown, Abu Yusuf rules the sale valid, while Muhammad rules it invalid.
Both are Hanafi imams; the dispute is intra-school.
4
Analyze the basis of each view
Abu Yusuf relies on the default assumption (al-asl baqa') that the item still exists. Muhammad argues that doubt in a condition (existence) prevents the condition from being satisfied, so ratification fails.
The core issue is whether doubt (shakk) invalidates a condition.
5
Connect to the marriage case
The passage notes that a suspended marriage is not invalidated by the death of the contractor. The heir can ratify if intercourse was not yet permissible.
This shows that the rule for sale does not apply to marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إجازته فلا ينفذ بإجازة غيره، فلو مات المالك لم ينفذ بإجازة الوارث بخلاف القسمة الموقوفة فإنها تنفذ بإجازة الوارث عند الثاني؛ كذا في البزازية . ولو لم يعلم حال المبيع وقت الإجازة من بقاء وعدمه جاز البيع في قول أبي يوسف، أو لا وهو قول محمد لأن الأصل بقاؤه ثم :لا رجع وقال : يصح ما لم يعلم قيامه عندهما لأن الشك وقع في شرط الإجازة فلا يثبت مع الشك. وقيد بالبيع لأن النكاح الموقوف لا يبطل بموت العاقد، ولو تزوجت أمة بغير إذن مولاها ثم مات المولى فإنه ينفذ بإجازة الوارث إذا لم يحل له وطؤها.
His ratification does not take effect by ratification of someone else. So if the owner dies, it does not take effect by ratification of the heir, unlike the suspended division, which does take effect by ratification of the heir according to the second. Likewise in al-Bazzaziyyah. If the condition of the sold property at the time of ratification regarding its existence or non-existence is unknown, the sale is valid according to Abu Yusuf, or not—which is the statement of Muhammad—because the basis is its existence. Then there is no recourse, and he said: It is valid as long as its existence is not known to the two of them, because doubt occurs in the condition of ratification, so it is not established with doubt. It is restricted to sale because the suspended marriage is not invalidated by the death of the contractor. If a slave girl marries without permission of her master, then the master dies, it takes effect by ratification of the heir if her intercourse is not permissible for him.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses two key issues in the sale by a fuduli (unauthorized agent) after the owner's death. First, the general rule: ratification is personal to the owner; his heir cannot ratify after his death, unlike the case of a suspended division (qisma mawqufa) where the heir can ratify according to the second view (Abu Yusuf). Second, if it is unknown whether the sold item still exists at the time of ratification, Abu Yusuf rules the sale valid because the default assumption is that the item exists, while Muhammad rules it invalid because ratification requires certainty of the item's existence to effect transfer of ownership. The dispute is based on whether doubt invalidates the condition of ratification. The text then distinguishes sale from marriage: a suspended marriage does not become void upon the death of the contracting party, and a slave girl's marriage without permission can be ratified by the heir if intercourse is not yet permissible.

Key Terms
فضولي (fudūlī): A person who acts without authorization, such as selling another's property without permission.
إجازة (ijāzah): Ratification of a suspended contract by the owner, making it effective retroactively.
موقوف (mawqūf): Suspended; a contract whose validity depends on subsequent ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a suspended marriage (nikah mawquf) when the person who contracted it dies?
A) It becomes void.
B) It is valid and requires ratification by heir.
C) It remains suspended and can be ratified by heir if intercourse not yet permissible.
D) It becomes automatically effective.

Key Takeaway
Ratification of a fuduli sale is personal to the owner and his death generally prevents heir ratification, except for division and marriage; the existence of the sold item must be certain for ratification according to Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf presumes it exists.

Forms and Effects of Ratification in a Fuduli Sale
Detailed rules on ratification forms and effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which actions and expressions constitute valid ratification (ijazah) in a fuduli sale and distinguish them from those that do not, and explain the buyer's right of rescission before ratification.

Core Idea
Ratification of a fuduli sale can be by deed or word, but not every word of praise counts; specific expressions like 'ahsanta' are not ratification, while 'bi'sa ma sana'ta' is.

The Hanafi school recognizes that a sale by a non-owner (fuduli) is suspended until the owner ratifies it. This lesson examines the precise forms of ratification—both by action and by speech—and clarifies common misconceptions about what constitutes consent. Understanding these rules is essential for determining when a fuduli sale becomes binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal context
Recognize that a fuduli sale is suspended and needs ratification to become binding. The buyer can rescind before ratification.
In marriage, the fuduli cannot rescind; the passage contrasts this.
2
Distinguish ratification by deed and by word
List actions that count as ratification: delivering the item, taking the price, gifting the price to the buyer. Then list words: some words count, others do not.
Action can speak louder than words.
3
Analyze specific expressions
Examine each expression mentioned: 'ahsanta', 'asabta', 'kafaytani mu'nat al-bay' are not ratification; 'bi'sa ma sana'ta' is ratification; 'ana radin' is ratification; 'imsakha' is not.
The underlying principle: does the expression imply acceptance of the contract as concluded?
4
Note exceptions and special cases
Silence is not ratification. Gift of price is ratification if the item still exists. The lessee's statement 'I do not ratify' does not bar later ratification, unlike in sale.
The lessee's situation shows that the effect of rejection can differ by contract type.
5
Apply the rules to a scenario
Construct a scenario and test which acts/words constitute ratification according to the passage.
Practice ensures understanding of subtle distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البزازية وللمشتري فسخ البيع قبل الإجازة تحرزاً عن لزوم العقد بخلاف الفضولي في النكاح ليس له أن يفسخ بالقول ولا بالفعل لأنه معبر محض فبالإجازة تنتقل العبارة إلى المالك فتصير الحقوق منوطة به لا بالفضولي. وفي النهاية أن الفضولي في النكاح يملك فسخه بالفعل بأن زوج فضولي رجلاً امرأة برضاها وقبل إجازته زوجه بأختها فإن ذلك يكون فسخاً للنكاح الأول. ثم الإجازة لبيع الفضولي يكون بالفعل وبالقول فمن الأول تسليم المبيع إجازة وكذا أخذه الثمن ومن الثاني طلب الثمن وقوله أحسنت أو وفقت أو أصبت ليس بإجازة، وكذا كفيتني مؤنة البيع أو أحسنت فجزاك الله خيراً. وفي المنتقى : لو قال بئس ما صنعت كان إجازة كقبض الثمن، ولو وهب المالك الثمن أو تصدق به على المشتري كان إجازة إن كان المبيع قائماً والسكوت بعد العلم لا يكون إجازة، ولو قال المالك أنا راض ما دمت حياً كان إجازة بالأول، ولو قال امسكها ما دمت حياً لا لأن الإمساك لا يدل على الرضا. وفي فروق الكرابيسي : أسأت إجازة. ولو قال لا أجيز يكون رداً للبيع بخلاف المستأجر إذا قال لا أجيز بيع الآجر ثم أجازه جاز.
And in al-Bazzaziyyah: The buyer may rescind the sale before ratification to avoid being bound by the contract, unlike the fuduli in marriage who has no right to rescind by word or deed because he is a pure intermediary; by ratification the right of speech transfers to the owner so rights become attached to him, not the fuduli. In al-Nihayah: The fuduli in marriage can rescind it by deed, such as if a fuduli marries a man to a woman with her consent, and before ratification he marries him to her sister, that constitutes a rescission of the first marriage. Then ratification of a fuduli's sale can be by deed or by word. As for the first: delivering the sold item is ratification, likewise taking the price. As for the second: demanding the price, and his saying 'well done' or 'you succeeded' or 'you hit the mark' is not ratification, likewise 'you saved me the trouble of selling' or 'well done, may Allah reward you'. In al-Muntaqa: If he said 'what a bad thing you did' that is ratification like taking the price. And if the owner gifts the price or gives it as charity to the buyer, it is ratification if the sold item exists, and silence after knowledge is not ratification. And if the owner said 'I am pleased as long as I live' it is ratification at the outset, and if he said 'keep it as long as I live' it is not because retention does not indicate consent. In Furuq al-Karabisi: 'You did wrong' is ratification. And if he said 'I do not ratify' it is a rejection of the sale, unlike the lessee if he said 'I do not ratify the lease' then later ratifies, it is valid.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq enumerates the detailed rules of what constitutes ratification (ijazah) of a fuduli's sale. First, the buyer may rescind before ratification to avoid being bound, unlike in marriage where the fuduli cannot rescind because he is merely an intermediary—ratification transfers authority to the owner. However, a fuduli in marriage can effectively rescind by marrying the same woman to someone else before ratification. Ratification of a sale can be by deed (like delivery of the item or receiving the price) or by word. But not all words of praise count; specific expressions like 'ahsanta' (well done) are not ratification, while 'bi'sa ma sana'ta' (what a bad thing you did) is, because it implies acceptance of the sale. Silence is not ratification. Words indicating consent like 'ana radin' (I am pleased) are ratification, while 'imsakha' (keep it) is not. If the owner says 'la ujiz' (I do not ratify), it is a rejection, but for the lessee (in a contract of lease) saying the same does not prevent later ratification—a distinction because in lease the contract is not suspended before performance.

Key Terms
إجازة بالفعل (ijāzah bi al-fi'l): Ratification by action, such as delivering the item or receiving the price.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of a contract before ratification; buyer can rescind to avoid being bound.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fuduli sale, which of the following is NOT considered ratification according to the passage?
A) The owner says 'bi'sa ma sana'ta'
B) The owner says 'ahsanta'
C) The owner takes the price from the buyer
D) The owner delivers the sold item to the buyer

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, ratification of a fuduli sale requires a clear indication of acceptance; not every word of praise counts, and silence or ambiguous retention does not suffice.

Effect of Ratification in Faduli Sale and Marriage
Explaining the effect of ratification in faduli sale and contrasting with marriage faduli

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the legal effect of ratification in a faduli sale, state the buyer's right to rescind, and distinguish the rules for faduli in marriage including the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Ratification in sale transforms faduli into agent; in marriage, faduli cannot rescind—ratification never makes him an agent.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the consequences of ratification in a faduli contract. In sale, ratification makes the faduli like an agent, transferring rights to him, and the buyer may rescind the contract before ratification to avoid being bound. In marriage, the faduli is a mere utterer and cannot rescind by word or deed, but there is a disagreement among later Hanafi authorities whether a conflicting marriage by the same faduli counts as rescission of the first.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Effect of Ratification in Sale
Locate the statement 'ذلك الإجازة يصير كالوكيل' – ratification makes the faduli like an agent. Understand that after ratification, the principal (owner) assumes rights and obligations of the contract.
2
Understand the Buyer's Right to Rescind Before Ratification
The buyer can rescind the sale before ratification ('للمشتري فسخ البيع قبل الإجازة') to avoid the contract becoming binding upon ratification. This is a protective right.
This is specific to sale; not applicable to marriage as per the main rule.
3
Contrast with Marriage Faduli: No Right to Rescind
The faduli in marriage cannot rescind by speech or action ('ليس له أن يفسخ بالقول ولا بالفعل') because he is merely an intermediary; rights attach to the owner after ratification.
4
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Rescission by Action
Note the two positions: al-Nihaya allows rescission by a subsequent conflicting marriage (e.g., marrying the same man to the woman's sister), while Qadikhan says this does not constitute rescission and the second marriage is also suspended.
This is an internal Hanafi ikhtilaf; the passage does not give a tarjih.
5
Analyze the Reasoning (Ta'lil)
The difference between sale and marriage is based on the nature of faduli's role: in sale, after ratification, rights revert to the faduli as agent; in marriage, the faduli is a mere 'mu'abbir' (communicator), so rights never attach to him.
This illustrates how Hanafi fiqh derives detailed rules from the underlying concept of direct agency vs. mere transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك الإجازة يصير كالوكيل فترجع حقوق العقد إليه فيطالب بالتسليم ويخاصم بالعيب وفي . ضرر به فله دفعه عن نفسه قبل ثبوته وفي البزازية وللمشتري فسخ البيع قبل الإجازة تحرزاً عن لزوم العقد بخلاف الفضولي في النكاح ليس له أن يفسخ بالقول ولا بالفعل لأنه معبر محض فبالإجازة تنتقل العبارة إلى المالك فتصير الحقوق منوطة به لا بالفضولي. وفي النهاية أن الفضولي في النكاح يملك فسخه بالفعل بأن زوج فضولي رجلاً امرأة برضاها وقبل إجازته زوجه بأختها فإن ذلك يكون فسخاً للنكاح الأول. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان : لا يكون فسخاً ويتوقف الثاني أيضاً.
That [ratification] makes him like an agent, so the rights of the contract revert to him, and he is demanded for delivery and sued for defects, and [the buyer] can repel harm from himself before its establishment. And in al-Bazzaziyya: The buyer may rescind the sale before ratification to avoid the binding of the contract, contrary to the faduli in marriage: he cannot rescind by speech or action because he is a mere utterer; by ratification, the utterance transfers to the owner, so rights are attached to him, not the faduli. And in al-Nihaya: The faduli in marriage can rescind it by action: if a faduli marries a man to a woman with her consent, and before ratification marries him to her sister, that is a rescission of the first marriage. And in Fatawa Qadikhan: It is not a rescission, and the second also becomes suspended.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary explaining the effect of ratification in faduli sale. It states that ratification transforms the faduli into an agent, so rights of the contract (like demanding delivery and suing for defects) revert to him. The buyer has the right to rescind before ratification to avoid the contract becoming binding. This is contrasted with faduli in marriage: the faduli cannot rescind by speech or action because he is a mere communicator. However, there is a dispute within the Hanafi school: according to al-Nihaya, the faduli in marriage can rescind by a subsequent action (e.g., marrying the same man to the woman's sister), while according to Qadikhan, it is not a rescission and the second marriage is also suspended. The author presents both views without explicit tarjih.

Key Terms
فضولي (fadūlī): A person who conducts a contract on behalf of another without authorization.
إجازة (ijāzah): Ratification by the owner of a contract made by a faduli, making it valid and binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens when the owner ratifies a faduli sale?
A) The faduli becomes the owner.
B) The faduli becomes like a wakil (agent).
C) The sale becomes void.
D) The buyer automatically loses the right to rescind.

Key Takeaway
Ratification in a faduli sale transforms the faduli into an agent with rights and obligations, but in marriage the faduli remains a mere intermediary with no power to rescind.

Forms of Ratification in Faduli Sale: Action and Speech
Listing what constitutes ratification by action and speech, including conditional statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which actions and verbal expressions constitute ratification (ijazah) in a faduli sale according to the Hanafi school, and distinguish between effective and ineffective statements.

Core Idea
Not every expression of approval is ratification—criticism may be, while praise may not be.

The Hanafi school provides detailed guidance on what counts as ratification in a faduli (unauthorized) sale. Ratification can be by action (such as delivering the item or taking the price) or by speech (such as demanding the price or even expressing disapproval in certain words). This lesson parses the key rulings from al-Bahr al-Raiq, helping you recognize valid and invalid forms of consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that ratification is divided into ratification by action (bi al-fi'l) and by speech (bi al-qawl). The passage explicitly states this division.
Actions can be as simple as delivering the sold item or taking the price.
2
List effective actions
From the passage: delivering the sold item and taking the price are clear actions of ratification. Also, giving the price as a gift or charity to the buyer is ratification if the sold item still exists.
Acts must clearly indicate consent.
3
Identify effective verbal statements
Demanding the price is verbal ratification. Surprisingly, saying 'bi'sa ma sana'ta' (what you did is bad) and 'asa'ta' (you did badly) are also ratification according to al-Muntaqa and Furq al-Karabisi.
Criticism can imply acceptance of the transaction.
4
Recognize ineffective statements
Words like 'ahsanta' (you did well), 'asabta' (you hit the mark), 'kafaytani mu'nat al-bay'' (you spared me trouble) are not ratification. Silence after knowledge is also not ratification.
Praise does not necessarily indicate consent to the contract.
5
Understand conditional and special cases
The owner's statement 'I am pleased as long as I live' is ratification. 'Keep it as long as I live' is not. Conditional ratification (e.g., 'I ratify if he sells for 100 dirhams') is valid only if the actual sale is for that type and not less; if more same type, it is valid according to Nawadir Hisham.
Conditions regarding price must be carefully examined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الإجازة لبيع الفضولي يكون بالفعل وبالقول فمن الأول تسليم المبيع إجازة وكذا أخذه الثمن ومن الثاني طلب الثمن وقوله أحسنت أو وفقت أو أصبت ليس بإجازة، وكذا كفيتني مؤنة البيع أو أحسنت فجزاك الله خيراً. وفي المنتقى : لو قال بئس ما صنعت كان إجازة كقبض الثمن، ولو وهب المالك الثمن أو تصدق به على المشتري كان إجازة إن كان المبيع قائماً والسكوت بعد العلم لا يكون إجازة، ولو قال المالك أنا راض ما دمت حياً كان إجازة بالأول، ولو قال امسكها ما دمت حياً لا لأن الإمساك لا يدل على الرضا. وفي فروق الكرابيسي : أسأت إجازة. ولو قال لا أجيز يكون رداً للبيع بخلاف المستأجر إذا قال لا أجيز بيع الآجر ثم أجازه جاز. وفي نوادر هشام: ولو قال أجزت إن باع بمائة درهم يجوز إن باع بأكثر، وإن باع بأقل لا يجوز ولو باع بألف دينار لا يجوز وإنما ينظر إلى النوع الذي وصفه ؛ كذا في البزازية.
Then ratification of a faduli sale is by action and speech. From the first: delivery of the sold item is ratification, likewise taking the price. From the second: demanding the price is ratification. His statement 'you did well' or 'you succeeded' or 'you hit the mark' is not ratification, likewise 'you spared me the trouble of selling' or 'you did well, may Allah reward you'. In al-Muntaqa: If he said 'what you did is bad' it is ratification like taking the price. And if the owner gives the price as a gift or charity to the buyer, it is ratification if the sold item is still in existence. Silence after knowledge is not ratification. And if the owner says 'I am pleased as long as I live' it is ratification in the first [interpretation]. If he says 'keep it as long as I live' it is not, because keeping does not indicate satisfaction. In Furq al-Karabisi: 'you did badly' is ratification. And if he says 'I do not ratify' it is rejection of the sale, contrary to the lessee if he says 'I do not ratify the sale of the hire' then later ratifies, it is valid. In Nawadir Hisham: If he says 'I ratify if he sells for a hundred dirhams' and he sells for more, it is valid; if he sells for less, it is not valid; and if he sells for a thousand dinars, it is not valid; rather, consideration is given to the type he described. Thus in al-Bazzaziyya.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage enumerates the forms of ratification in faduli sale. It distinguishes between ratification by action (like delivering the sold item or taking the price) and by speech (like demanding the price). Specific expressions are listed: words like 'ahsanta' (you did well) are not ratification, while 'bi'sa ma sana'ta' (what you did is bad) is ratification, as per al-Muntaqa. Gifting or giving in charity the price to the buyer is ratification if the object still exists. Silence after knowledge is not ratification. The statement 'I am pleased as long as I live' is ratification, but 'keep it as long as I live' is not because it implies permission to hold, not satisfaction. Interestingly, 'asa'ta' (you did badly) is also ratification according to al-Karabisi. Rejection words like 'la ujizu' (I do not ratify) are effective as rejection, unless the person is a lessee (musta'jir) who can later change his mind. Conditional ratification: if the owner says 'I ratify if he sells for 100 dirhams', the ratification is valid only if the actual sale is for that type of price (e.g., in dirhams) and not for less or for a different currency, according to Nawadir Hisham.

Key Terms
بيع الفضولي (bay' al-fadūlī): A sale conducted by an unauthorized person on behalf of the owner.
إجازة بالفعل (ijāzah bi al-fi'l): Ratification by action, such as delivering the goods or taking the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ratification can be indicated by action or speech; specific expressions like 'bista ma sana'ta' are considered ratification, while praise words like 'ahsanta' are not.
