The Faduli's Authority to Reduce Price After Ratification and the Owner's Option
Discussing the faduli's authority to reduce the price after ratification and the owner's option if unaware

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a faduli can reduce the price after ratification and explain the owner's option if unaware of the reduction.

Core Idea
After ratification, the faduli becomes an agent; his price reduction is valid but the owner may rescind if unaware.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq discusses a nuanced scenario in faduli sales. After the owner ratifies the sale, the faduli is treated as an agent, allowing him to reduce the price. However, to protect the owner, if he only learns of the reduction later, he has an option to accept or cancel the sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Find the two distinct legal statements in the text: (1) the faduli's reduction of the price is valid after ratification, and (2) the owner has an option (khiyar) if he later learns of the reduction.
These rulings come from two different sources cited in the passage.
2
Understand the principle behind the first ruling
Ask: Why does the faduli gain authority to reduce the price? Because ratification retroactively confirms the faduli's actions, treating him as an agent (وكيل) from the beginning.
Agency (wakala) grants the agent authority to negotiate within bounds.
3
Contrast the two sources
Al-Bazzaziyya states the reduction is valid 'whether the owner knew the price or not.' Al-Hidayah adds the owner's option upon later knowledge. Identify the addition and its protective purpose.
The option protects the owner from being bound to a reduction he did not authorize.
4
Apply to a concrete scenario
Imagine a faduli sells a car for $10,000, the owner ratifies, then the faduli reduces the price to $9,000. The owner later discovers this. Determine the legal outcome using the two rulings.
The reduction is valid, but the owner may accept or rescind.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Identify which position is relied upon for fatwa. The owner's option is the mufta bihi, as it balances the faduli's authority with the owner's right.
This is consistent with Hanafi preference for protecting the owner's interest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيها : وإذا أجاز المالك بيع الفضولي صار الفضولي كالوكيل حتى صح حطه عن الثمن علم المالك بالثمن أو لم يعلم. وأجاب صاحب الهداية أنه إذا علم بالحط بعد الإجازة فله الخيار إن شاء رضي به وإن شاء فسخ اهـ.
And in it [al-Bazzaziyya]: When the owner ratifies a faduli sale, the faduli becomes like an agent so that his reduction of the price is valid, whether the owner knew the price or not. And the author of al-Hidayah answered that if he (the owner) becomes aware of the reduction after ratification, he has an option: if he wishes he may accept it, and if he wishes he may rescind.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific ruling: after the owner ratifies a faduli sale, the faduli is treated like an agent. Consequently, if the faduli reduces the price (i.e., agrees to a lower price than originally set), that reduction is valid even if the owner was unaware of the original price or the reduction. However, according to al-Hidayah, if the owner later learns of the reduction, he has an option (khiyar): he may accept the reduced price and affirm the sale, or he may rescind the contract. This protects the owner's interest while recognizing the faduli's authority post-ratification.

Key Terms
حط (hatt): Reduction of the price by the faduli after ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The sale becomes invalid.

Key Takeaway
After ratification, a faduli can reduce the price, but if the owner later learns of the reduction, he has an option to accept or rescind.

Guarantee Establishes Ownership: Not a Faduli Sale
Critical refinement: guarantee establishes ownership, resolving a textual contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a sale by a non-owner who later guarantees is considered the owner's sale, and reconcile the conflicting rulings on buyer's rescission before ratification.

Core Idea
Paying guarantee makes the seller owner, so the sale is not faduli—it is the owner's sale.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a subtle but important point: when a person sells something they don't own but later compensates the owner (damān), the sale is not a suspended (faduli) sale—it becomes the owner's sale. The author then resolves an apparent contradiction between two texts about the buyer's right to rescind before ratification, showing that the rulings apply to different factual scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The passage addresses a sale by a non-owner who later pays guarantee. Understand why this is different from a faduli sale.
2
Understand the legal effect of guarantee
According to the Hanafi school, paying damān retrospectively establishes ownership for the seller. Therefore, the sale becomes the owner's sale, not a suspended contract.
This is analogous to a usurper (ghasib) who sells and the owner guarantees him.
3
Recognize the support from senior scholars
The author notes that this view is supported by the author of al-Nahr and appears in most Hanafi works like al-Bazzaziyya.
This reinforces the reliability of the position.
4
Analyze the apparent contradiction on buyer's rescission
One text allows the buyer to rescind before ratification; another text says if the buyer proves seller's lack of authorization, the rescission is not accepted. The resolution lies in differing underlying assumptions: one case where both agree the sale was unauthorized, the other where there is a dispute.
The key is to identify the subject difference.
5
Apply the clarification
When encountering such rulings, always consider the factual context: does the seller later guarantee? Do the parties agree on authorization?
This prevents misapplication of the rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيع من مسائل الفضولي بل هي بيع المالك لأنه بالضمان استند الملك ونفذ البيع من جهته كبيع الغاصب إذا ضمنه المالك كما هو ظاهر والمسألة مذكورة في غالب كتب المذهب كالبزازية وغيرها والله تعالى أعلم. ثم رأيت صاحب النهر تكلم بمثل ما تكلمته .اهـ. وعبارة النهر : وليس هذا من إجازة الفضولي في شيء بل إنما نفذ بيعه لثبوت الملك للبائع بأداء الضمان ضرورة فلا استثناء حينئذ فتدبره قوله : (وفي البزازية وللمشتري فسخ البيع قبل الإجازة الخ إن قلت يأباه ما سيأتي في المتن أن المشتري إذا برهن على إقرار البائع أو رب العبد أنه لم يأمره بالبيع وأراد رد البيع لم يقبل . من قلت : لا تنافي بينهما لأن ما سيأتي مفروض فيما إذا اختلف البائع والمشتري فادعى المشتري أن البيع بغير أمر صاحبه وجحد البائع ذلك، فيحمل ما في البزازية على ما إذا تصادقا على البيع بغير أمر المالك فاختلف الموضوع فافهم حاشية أبي السعود.
This is not from the issues of faduli; rather it is a sale by the owner because ownership is established by guarantee and the sale is executed from his side, like the sale of a usurper when the owner guarantees him, as is apparent. The issue is mentioned in most books of the school like al-Bazzaziyya and others, and Allah knows best. Then I saw the author of al-Nahr speak similarly to what I said. The text of al-Nahr: This is not at all from the ratification of faduli; rather his sale is executed due to ownership being established for the seller by paying the guarantee out of necessity, so there is no exception then, so ponder it. His statement: (In al-Bazzaziyya: The buyer can rescind the sale before ratification...) If you say, this is contradicted by what will come in the matn: that if the buyer proves the seller's admission or the master's admission that he did not order the sale, and wants to return the sale, it is not accepted. I say: There is no contradiction, because what will come is assumed when the seller and buyer disagree, the buyer claims the sale was without the owner's order and the seller denies it. So what is in al-Bazzaziyya is interpreted as when they both agree that the sale was without the owner's order, so the subject differs. Understand from the Hashiya of Abi al-Sa'ud.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a clarification and critical refinement. First, the author notes that a sale where the seller (who initially did not own the item) later guarantees it (i.e., compensates the owner) is not a faduli sale at all. Rather, it becomes a sale by the owner because ownership is established by the guarantee. This is analogous to a usurper (ghasib) who sells and then the owner guarantees him, making the sale valid from the owner's side. The author of al-Nahr confirms this. Second, the commentator resolves a seeming contradiction: In al-Bazzaziyya, it says the buyer can rescind before ratification. But later in the matn (the original text), it says that if the buyer presents proof that the seller admitted he was not authorized, and wants to return the sale, it is not accepted. The commentator explains that the later case is when the seller and buyer disagree about authorization, so the seller's denial is given weight. The earlier case is when both agree that the sale was without authorization, so the buyer can rescind before ratification. This reconciliation is from the Hashiya of Abi al-Sa'ud.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Guarantee or compensation paid by the seller to the owner, establishing the seller's ownership retrospectively.
بيع فضولي (bayʿ fudūlī): A sale by a person who is not the owner and has no authorization; such a sale is suspended pending ratification by the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can a buyer rescind the sale before ratification?
A) Whenever the buyer discovers the seller was not the owner.
B) Only when the seller and buyer disagree about whether the seller had authorization.
C) Only when both the seller and buyer agree that the sale was without the owner's order.
D) Only after the owner has ratified the sale.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, paying guarantee turns a non-owner's sale into an owner's sale, and the buyer's right to rescind depends on whether both parties agree about the lack of authorization.

Partial Receipt of Price as Ratification (Ramli's View)
Ruling on partial price receipt as ratification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state al-Ramli's ruling that partial receipt of the price constitutes ratification and explain his three supporting evidences.

Core Idea
Partial receipt of the price can constitute ratification because it indicates consent, analogous to partial dowry receipt in marriage.

Al-Ramli identifies a gap in earlier Hanafi texts: whether taking only part of the sale price by the owner of a faduli sale counts as ratification. He argues it should, based on indication of satisfaction, analogy to marriage, and the generic meaning of the definite article.

Guided Path
1
Identify the missing ruling
Al-Ramli notes that earlier scholars did not address whether receiving part of the price amounts to ratification. Recognize that this is an undecided case (maskut anhu).
2
State al-Ramli's conclusion
He says 'yenbaghi an yakuna ijazah' – it should be considered ratification. This is his reasoned opinion.
Note the cautious language: 'should be', not definitive.
3
Examine the first evidence: indication of satisfaction
Receiving part of the price demonstrates the owner's willingness to accept the sale, which is the essence of ratification.
4
Examine the second evidence: analogy to marriage
In faduli marriage, receiving part of the dowry is considered ratification. By analogy (qiyas), the same applies to sale.
The analogy is based on the common element: acceptance of a benefit indicates consent.
5
Examine the third evidence: linguistic analysis
The definite article 'al' in 'al-thaman' is for the genus, meaning any part of the price falls under the general term, so partial receipt is covered.
This textual interpretation supports the broad application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وكذا أخذه الثمن) قال الرملي: لم أر في كلامهم حكم ما إذا قبض بعض الثمن هل يكون إجازة أم لا، وينبغي أن يكون إجازة لدلالته على الرضا، ولتصريحهم في نكاح الفضولي بأن قبض بعض المهر يكون إجازة، ولأن الظاهر أن الألف واللام في الثمن لإفادة الجنس المحرره الغزي .اهـ.
His statement: (And likewise taking the price) al-Ramli said: I have not seen in their statements the ruling regarding when he takes part of the price, whether it is ratification or not. It should be ratification because it indicates satisfaction, and because they explicitly stated in faduli marriage that taking part of the dowry is ratification, and because the apparent meaning is that the definite article in 'al-thaman' is for the genus. Thus wrote al-Ghazzi.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This is a comment by al-Ramli on the phrase 'وكذا أخذه الثمن' (and likewise taking the price) from the earlier discussion of forms of ratification. Al-Ramli notes that the scholars did not explicitly address whether taking only part of the price constitutes ratification. He suggests that it should be considered ratification because it indicates the owner's satisfaction. He draws an analogy from the case of faduli marriage where taking part of the dowry (mahr) is considered ratification. Additionally, the definite article 'al' in 'al-thaman' (the price) is for the genus, meaning any part of the price would fall under the general rule. Al-Ghazzi endorsed this view. Thus, according to al-Ramli, partial receipt of the price is sufficient as ratification.

Key Terms
قبض بعض الثمن (qabḍ ba‘ḍ al-thaman): Receiving part of the price by the owner as an indication of ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Partial receipt of dowry in faduli marriage
B) Full receipt of price in a normal sale
C) Partial receipt of rent in a lease
D) Full receipt of gift in a hibah

Key Takeaway
Al-Ramli's view is that partial receipt of the price constitutes ratification, based on indication of satisfaction, analogy to marriage, and the generic meaning of the definite article.

Condition of Qiyām al-Mabīʿ for Valid Ratification
Commentator explains the condition of qiyām al-mabīʿ with three examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition that the sold object must remain in existence for ratification and distinguish between transformations that invalidate ratification and those that do not.

Core Idea
Ratification requires the object to remain in its original state; only substantial transformation (e.g., dyeing) invalidates it, not changes like birth or demolition.

In Hanafi fiqh, a sale conducted by a non‑owner (fudūlī) is suspended until the owner ratifies it. But what if the object changes before ratification? This passage from al‑Baḥr al‑Rāʾiq explains the key condition of qiyām al‑mabīʿ (the sold item remaining in existence) and illustrates it with three concrete cases. Understanding this rule ensures that the owner’s ratification is truly meaningful—they approve exactly what was sold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Condition for Valid Ratification
The passage states that the author conditioned ratification on the sold item remaining (qiyām al-mabīʿ) both in name (bi-ismihi) and state (bi-ḥālihi). Recognize this as a shart (condition) for the validity of ijāza.
In Hanafi terminology, 'name' refers to the legal category of the object, while 'state' refers to its specific attributes.
2
Analyze the Three Illustrative Cases
The commentator gives three scenarios: dyeing a garment, a slave‑girl giving birth, and a house being demolished. For each, determine whether ratification is valid or not based on whether the object's 'essence' remains.
Dyeing: the cloth acquires a new identity (it becomes a dyed cloth). Birth: the mother remains the same, so ratification is valid and the child follows the mother. Demolition: the land (‘arṣa) remains, so the essence persists.
3
Derive the Underlying Principle
The key distinction is between changes that alter the object’s identity (substantial transformation) and changes that do not (additions or partial destruction). Only substantial transformation prevents ratification.
Substantial transformation means the object no longer belongs to the same genus or its core attributes are lost. Dyeing changes the cloth fundamentally; giving birth is an addition to the mother; demolition removes the building but leaves the land.
4
Apply the Rule to New Scenarios
Practice identifying whether ratification would be valid in hypotheticals, such as a car being repainted (likely not substantial) or wheat being ground into flour (substantial).
The illa (effective cause) is the change in the object's identity, not mere change in condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأشار المؤلف باشتراط قيام المبيع إلى قوله المصنف باشتراط قيام المبيع أي باسمه وحاله إلى أنه لو أجازه بعد صبغ الثوب المشتري فإنه لا يجوز ولو ولدت الأمة ثم أجاز المالك البيع يكون الولد مع الأمة للمشتري، ولو انهدم الدار ثم أجاز المالك البيع يصح لبقاء العرصة.
His statement: (And the author indicated the condition of the sold item remaining) to his statement: The author [conditioned] the sold item remaining, i.e., in its name and state, to the extent that if he ratified after the buyer had dyed the garment, it is not valid. And if the slave-girl gave birth, then the owner ratified the sale, the child together with the mother is for the buyer. And if the house was demolished, then the owner ratified the sale, it is valid due to the land remaining.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition that the sold object must remain in existence at the time of ratification. The commentator explains the author's indication that the sold item must be 'qa'im' (existing). Examples: If the buyer dyes a garment (changing its nature), ratification is not valid because the object has been transformed. If a slave-girl gives birth, then ratification is valid and the child goes with the mother to the buyer because the mother remains in existence (the birth does not eliminate the original). If a house is demolished, ratification is still valid because the land ('arsa) remains, which is the essential part of the sale. Thus, substantial transformation that changes the object's identity (like dyeing) prevents ratification, while partial changes or destruction of buildings do not prevent it as long as the essence remains.

Key Terms
قيام المبيع (qiyām al-mabī‘): The requirement that the sold item exists in its original form at the time of ratification.
البيع الفضولي (al‑bayʿ al‑fudūlī): A sale conducted by a person who is not the owner, which is suspended pending ratification by the owner.
إجازة (ijāza): Ratification by the owner of a contract made by a non‑owner, which validates the contract retrospectively.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following makes ratification invalid?
A) The slave-girl gives birth.
B) The house is demolished.
C) The buyer dyes the garment.
D) The buyer uses the garment.

Key Takeaway
For a fudūlī sale ratification, the object must remain in its original identity; substantial transformation (like dyeing) invalidates ratification, but changes that do not alter the essence (like birth or demolition) are permissible.

Liability When Faduli Delivers and Object Perishes: Owner's Option
Ruling on liability when the faduli delivers the object and it is destroyed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the owner's options when the faduli's delivered object perishes and determine the validity of the sale based on the owner's choice, distinguishing between the author's view and Muhammad's view.

Core Idea
The owner claims from either buyer or faduli; claiming from buyer invalidates the sale.

This lesson expands on a scenario not explicitly covered by the author: what happens if the faduli (unauthorized agent) delivers the sold item and it perishes. The owner has a choice, and his choice determines the legal outcome. We will examine the owner's option, the consequences for the sale, and an intra-Hanafi difference on whether the sale can be valid when the faduli's possession was trust.

Guided Path
1
Identify the owner's right
State that the owner can claim compensation from either the buyer or the faduli. Once he chooses, the other is freed.
This is because compensation is a form of ownership transfer.
2
Analyze claiming from the buyer
If the owner claims from the buyer, the sale becomes invalid. The buyer then recovers the price from the faduli, not the compensation he paid.
Taking the value is like taking the object itself.
3
Analyze claiming from the faduli
If the owner claims from the faduli, the validity of the sale depends on the nature of the faduli's possession before delivery. Distinguish between guarantee (mamun 'alayh) and trust (amanah).
The author says sale is valid only if possession was guarantee; Muhammad says it is valid even if trust.
4
Compare intra-Hanafi views
The author holds that if the faduli's possession was trust, the sale is not executed because the cause of ownership (liability) comes after the contract. Muhammad holds that the sale is valid by analogy to a usurper.
Muhammad's view is from Zahir al-Riwaya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المؤلف حكم تسليم المبيع من الفضولي فلو سلمه فهلك فللمالك أن يضمن أيهما شاء فأيهما اختار ضمانه برئ الآخر لأن في التضمين تمليكاً منه فإذا ملكه من أحدهما لا يمكن تمليكه من الآخر، فإن اختار تضمين المشتري بطل البيع لأن أخذ القيمة كأخذ العين ويرجع المشتري على البائع بالثمن لا بما ضمن، وإن اختار تضمين البائع ينظر؛ إن كان قبض البائع مضموناً عليه نفذ بيعه بالضمان لأن ملکه قد عقده، وإن كان قبضه أمانة فإنما صار مضموناً عليه بالتسليم بعد البيع فلا ينفذ بيعه تم بالضمان لتأخر سبب ملکه عن العقد. وقد ذكر محمد في ظاهر الرواية أنه يجوز البيع بتضمين البائع، ووجهه سلم أولاً ثم صار مضموناً عليه ثم باعه فصار كالمغصوب ؛ كذا في البزازية
And the author did not mention the ruling of delivery of the sold item by the faduli. If he delivers it and it perishes, the owner can claim compensation from whichever he wishes. Whichever he chooses to claim from, the other is freed, because compensation is a form of ownership transfer from him; so if he makes one owner, it is not possible to make the other owner. If he chooses to claim from the buyer, the sale becomes invalid because taking the value is like taking the object itself, and the buyer has recourse against the seller for the price, not for what he guaranteed. If he chooses to claim from the seller, consideration is given: if the seller's possession was guarantee (mamun 'alayh), his sale is executed by the guarantee because his ownership has been contracted; if his possession was trust (amanah), then it becomes guarantee only by delivery after the sale, so his sale is not executed by the guarantee due to the delay of the cause of his ownership after the contract. And Muhammad mentioned in Zahir al-Riwaya that the sale is valid with the seller being claimed from; its reasoning: he first took possession, then became liable, then sold, so it becomes like a usurped item. Thus in al-Bazzaziyya.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario not covered by the author: delivery of the sold item by the faduli. If the faduli delivers the item and it is destroyed, the owner has the right to claim compensation from either the buyer or the faduli. Once he chooses one, the other is discharged. If he claims from the buyer, the sale becomes invalid because taking the value (qima) is like taking the object itself. The buyer then recovers the price from the seller (faduli). If he claims from the faduli, the validity of the sale depends on the nature of the faduli's possession before the sale. If the faduli was already in guarantee possession (e.g., he was a usurper), then the sale is valid because his ownership was established by the guarantee. If his possession was trust (e.g., he was a bailee), then his liability arises only after delivery, so the sale is not valid based on the guarantee because the cause of ownership (the guarantee) comes after the contract. Muhammad al-Shaybani, in Zahir al-Riwaya, holds that the sale is valid by claiming from the seller, based on the order of events: he first had trust possession, then became liable by delivery, then sold, making it like a usurped item.

Key Terms
تضمين (taḍmīn): Claiming compensation (damages) from either the buyer or the faduli when the object is destroyed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the owner chooses to claim compensation from the buyer?
A) The sale remains valid.
B) The sale becomes invalid.
C) The faduli must also compensate.
D) The buyer can keep the object.

Key Takeaway
The owner's choice of whom to claim compensation from determines the validity of the faduli's sale.

Purchasing Property for Another Without Agency
Ruling on contract attribution without agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi default rule that buying for another without agency vests ownership in the buyer, and apply the correct ruling when the contract is attributed to the buyer or to the third party.

Core Idea
Buying for another without agency makes the buyer the owner; ratification only affects a suspended contract.

When someone purchases property for another person without being an authorized agent, the default ruling is that the property belongs to the buyer himself. However, depending on how the contract is worded—whether the buyer attributes the contract to himself or to the intended beneficiary—the purchase may be suspended on the latter's ratification. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position on contract attribution and the legal effect of subsequent ratification or denial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is purchasing property for another without prior agency. The default ruling is that the purchase is for the buyer himself, regardless of the buyer's intention. Ratification by the intended beneficiary does not retroactively change ownership.
This is a general rule unless the contract is expressed in a way that makes it suspended.
2
Distinguish by contract attribution
If the buyer says 'I bought from you for so-and-so' (attributing the contract to himself but mentioning the beneficiary), the purchase is suspended on so-and-so's ratification. If the buyer says 'Sell this to so-and-so' (attributing the contract to the third party), the sale is also suspended. But if the seller says 'I sold to you for so-and-so', the purchase is for the addressee (the buyer), not for so-and-so.
The key is whether the contract is attributed to the buyer or to the third party in the offer or acceptance.
3
Analyze the effect of subsequent ratification
If the buyer buys a slave and declares to witnesses that he is buying for so-and-so, and so-and-so later says 'I am satisfied', the contract remains for the buyer because he was not an agent at the time of contract. Ratification applies only to suspended contracts, not to executed ones.
Ratification cannot change ownership that has already vested in the buyer.
4
Handle disputes between parties
If the buyer and the alleged principal later disagree: the principal claims the purchase was by his order and ownership fell to him, while the buyer denies it. The statement of the principal is given weight because the purchase is by his admission for him.
This is based on the principle that one's admission is evidence against himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بالبيع لأنه إذا اشترى لغيره كان ما اشتراه لنفسه أجاز الذي اشتراه له أم لا . وإن لم يجد نفاذاً يتوقف على إجازة من المشتري له كالصبي المحجور يشتري شيئاً لغيره فيتوقف هذا إذا أضاف العقد إلى نفسه، أما إذا أضافه إلى غيره بأن يقول بع هذا العبد لفلان فقال البائع بعته لفلان يتوقف على أجازته، وأما إذا قال اشتريت منك بكذا الأجل فلان فقال البائع بعت أو قال البائع بعت منك لفلان فإنه يقع الشراء للمخاطب لا لفلان والصحيح أنه إذا أضيف العقد في أحد الكلامين إلى يتوقف على إجازة فلان ولو اشترى عبداً وأشهد أنه يشتريه لفلان وقال فلان رضيت فالعقد للمشتري لأنه إذا لم يكن وكيلاً بالشراء وقع الملك له فلا اعتبار بالإجازة بعد ذلك وهي تلحق العقد الموقوف لا النافذ، فإن دفع المشتري إليه العبد وأخذ الثمن كان بيعاً بالتعاطي بينهما، ولو ظن المشتري والمشتري له أن الملك وقع للمشتري له فسلمه له بعد قبض ثمنه لا يسترد بلا رضا المشتري له ويجعل كأنه ولاه وإن علما أن الشراء وقع للمشتري بعده وإن زعم المشتري له أن الشراء كان بأمره ووقع الملك له والمشتري أنه كان بلا أمره ووقع الشراء للمشتري فالقول للمشتري له لأن الشراء بإقراره وقع له؛ كذا في البزازية.
And it is restricted to sale because if he buys for someone else, what he bought is for himself, whether the person he bought it for ratifies or not. And if there is no execution, it is suspended on the ratification of the person he bought it for, like a minor under interdiction who buys something for someone else, it is suspended. This is when he attributes the contract to himself. As for when he attributes it to someone else, by saying 'sell this slave to so-and-so' and the seller says 'I sold it to so-and-so', it is suspended on his ratification. And if he says 'I bought from you for so-and-so for such price' and the seller says 'I sold' or the seller says 'I sold to you for so-and-so', then the purchase occurs for the addressee, not for so-and-so. And the correct view is that if the contract is attributed in one of the two statements to so-and-so, it is suspended on so-and-so's ratification. And if he buys a slave and bears witness that he buys it for so-and-so, and so-and-so says 'I am satisfied', the contract is for the buyer because if he was not an agent, ownership falls to him; so ratification after that is not considered, and it applies to a suspended contract, not an executed one. If the buyer then gives the slave to him and takes the price, it is a sale by hand-to-hand transaction (mu'atāt). And if both the buyer and the one for whom it was bought think that ownership fell to the latter, so he hands it over after receipt of its price, it cannot be recovered without the latter's consent, and it is as if he had transferred it to him. And if they both know that the purchase fell to the buyer, later he [the buyer] claims that the purchase was by his order and ownership fell to him, while the buyer claims it was without his order and the purchase fell to the buyer, then the statement of the one for whom it was bought is given weight because the purchase is by his admission for him. Thus in al-Bazzaziyya.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario of purchasing property for another person without being an authorized agent. The general rule is that when a person buys something for someone else (without agency), the purchase is for himself unless the person for whom he intended to buy ratifies. This is akin to a faduli sale. However, the ruling depends on how the contract is expressed. If the buyer says 'I buy this for so-and-so' but attributes the contract to himself (e.g., 'I bought from you for so-and-so'), the purchase is suspended on the ratification of so-and-so. If the buyer attributes the contract entirely to the third party (e.g., 'Sell this to so-and-so'), the sale is also suspended on the third party's ratification. But if the seller says 'I sold to you for so-and-so', the purchase is for the addressee (the buyer himself), not for so-and-so. An interesting case: If a buyer purchases a slave and declares that he is buying it for so-and-so, and so-and-so later expresses satisfaction, the contract is still for the buyer because he was not an agent at the time of contract; ratification after the fact does not change ownership. The buyer can then transfer ownership by a separate transaction (e.g., hand-to-hand sale). The passage also covers a dispute scenario: if the parties later disagree about whether the purchase was authorized, the statement of the alleged principal is given weight over the buyer's claim.

Key Terms
الشراء لغيره (al-shirā' li-ghayrihi): Purchasing property for another person without being an agent.
إجازة (ijāzah): Ratification; a subsequent approval of a contract that makes it binding.
عقد موقوف (ʿaqd mawqūf): A suspended contract that becomes binding upon ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer says 'I bought from you for so-and-so' and the seller says 'I sold', what is the ruling on ownership?
A) The purchase is for so-and-so immediately.
B) The purchase is for the buyer immediately.
C) The purchase is suspended on so-and-so's ratification.
D) The purchase is void.

Key Takeaway
Without agency, a purchase for another vests ownership in the buyer; only a suspended contract can be transferred by ratification.

The Effect of Dyeing on Ratification: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Resolving conflict about ratification after dyeing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi views on ratification after dyeing a garment and explain the reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
Dyeing does not change identity; cutting and sewing does.

When a buyer dyes a garment before the owner ratifies the sale, does the owner's subsequent ratification validate the contract? Hanafi scholars differ. This lesson dissects the conflict between al-Bazzaziyya and Manh al-Ghaffar, focusing on what counts as a change of identity (taghayyur) that prevents ratification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the transformation
Determine what the buyer did to the garment: dyeing (ṣabgh) vs. cutting and sewing (qaṭ' wa khiyāṭa).
Dyeing only changes color; cutting and sewing creates a new garment.
2
State the two conflicting rulings
Al-Bazzaziyya holds ratification invalid after dyeing. Manh al-Ghaffar holds it valid after dyeing, but invalid after cutting and sewing.
The conflict is only about dyeing; both agree cutting/sewing prevents ratification.
3
Understand the underlying criterion
The key is whether the garment remains 'the same thing' (qā'im) in the eyes of custom. Dyeing does not alter identity; cutting and sewing does.
This is based on the Fatawa of Abu al-Layth as recorded in al-Tatarkhaniyya.
4
Note the authority level
Al-Bazzaziyya is a later fatwa collection, Manh al-Ghaffar is from the same period. The passage does not explicitly declare a mufta bihi, but citing Abu al-Layth suggests Manh al-Ghaffar's view is stronger.
Tarjih may depend on context and custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو أجازه بعد صبغ الثوب المشتري فإنه لا يجوز كذا في البزازية، وفي منح الغفار ما يخالفه فإنه قال : والمراد بكون المبيع قائماً أن لا يكون متغيراً بحيث يعد شيئاً آخر فإنه لو باع ثوب غيره بغير أمره وصبغه المشتري فأجاز رب الثوب البيع جاز ، ولو قطعه وحاطه ثم أجاز البيع لا يجوز لأنه صار شيئاً آخر اهـ. والمسألة بهذا اللفظ دون التعليل في التتارخانية عن فتاوى أبي الليث. قوله: (والصحيح أنه إذا أضيف العقد في أحد الكلامين إلى فلان يتوقف الخ) ظاهره أنه يتوقف وإن أضيف في الكلام الآخر إلى الفضولي ويأتي قريباً أن أصح الروايتين في هذه الصورة أنه
If he ratifies after the buyer has dyed the garment, it is not valid, as in al-Bazzaziyya. But in Manh al-Ghaffar, there is something that opposes it: he said: The meaning of the sold item being 'existing' is that it has not changed such that it is considered something else. So if someone sells another's garment without his permission, and the buyer dyes it, then the owner of the garment ratifies the sale, it is valid. But if he cuts it and sews it, then ratifies, it is not valid because it has become something else. The issue is mentioned with this wording without the reasoning in al-Tatarkhaniyya from the Fatawa of Abu al-Layth. His statement: (And the correct view is that if the contract is attributed in one of the two statements to so-and-so, it is suspended...) Its apparent meaning is that it is suspended even if it is attributed in the other statement to the faduli. And it will come shortly that the more correct of the two narrations in this scenario is that...
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents a conflict in the Hanafi school regarding ratification after the buyer has dyed the garment (a substantial transformation). According to al-Bazzaziyya, ratification is not valid after dyeing because the object has changed. However, in Manh al-Ghaffar, it is said that dyeing does not prevent ratification because the garment is still considered the same object despite color change; only cutting and sewing (which creates a new garment) prevents ratification. This is reported from Abu al-Layth's Fatawa. The commentator then records a hashya on the earlier rule about contract attribution to a third party: the correct view is that if the contract is attributed to so-and-so in one of the two statements (e.g., the buyer says 'I buy for so-and-so'), it is suspended on so-and-so's ratification, even if the other statement attributes it to the faduli. The note says that the more correct of two narrations on this will come shortly, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
صبغ الثوب (ṣabgh al-thawb): Dyeing a garment, which some consider a transformation that prevents ratification, others not.
قائماً (qā'iman): Existing; the condition for ratification that the sold item remains essentially the same.
شيئاً آخر (shay'an ākhar): Something else; the point at which the item has changed identity and ratification is no longer possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) After dyeing.
B) After cutting and sewing.
C) After any transformation.
D) Never after transformation.

Key Takeaway
The validity of ratification after the buyer transforms the item depends on whether the item remains essentially the same; dyeing does not change identity, but cutting and sewing does.

Qiyam al-Mabi': The Condition of the Sold Item's Existence for Fuduli Ratification
Explaining the requirement for the sold item to remain unchanged for ratification of a fuduli sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of qiyam al-mabi' to determine whether a fuduli sale can be ratified after changes to the sold item.

Core Idea
Ratification of a fuduli sale requires the sold item to remain fundamentally unchanged at the time of ratification.

A fuduli contract is suspended until the owner ratifies, but the item must be in its original state. The passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq gives three illustrative examples: dyeing (a fundamental change) invalidates ratification, while birth (an increase) and house collapse (partial perishing) do not, as the item's essential identity remains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The author of Kanz al-Daqaiq requires qiyam al-mabi' – the sold item must exist in its name and condition at the time of ratification.
This is a condition for the validity of ratification.
2
Analyze the three examples
The passage provides three distinct cases: dyeing (fundamental change), birth (increase), and house collapse (partial perishing). For each, determine whether the item remains substantially the same.
Focus on the legal outcome: valid or invalid ratification.
3
Distinguish substantial change from mere increase or reduction
Dyeing changes the cloth into a different item (ṣāra shayʾan ākhar). Birth is an increase that does not alter the mother. House collapse leaves the land (ʿarṣa), which is part of the sold property.
The key is whether the item's essence (ʿayn) has been transformed.
4
Apply the principle
If the item is fundamentally altered, ratification is invalid; if the same substance remains with increase or reduction of parts, ratification is valid.
This applies to both movables and immovables.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع/ فصل في بيع الفضولي المصنف باشتراط قيام المبيع أي باسمه وحاله إلى أنه لو أجازه بعد صبغ الثوب المشتري فإنه لا يجوز ولو ولدت الأمة ثم أجاز المالك البيع يكون الولد مع الأمة للمشتري، ولو انهدم الدار ثم أجاز المالك البيع يصح لبقاء العرصة.
The Book of Sales / Chapter on the Sale of the Unauthorized Agent. The author requires the existence of the sold item, i.e. in its name and condition, to the extent that if he ratifies after the buyer dyes the garment, it is not valid. If the slave-girl gives birth and then the owner ratifies the sale, the child together with the mother goes to the buyer. If the house collapses and then the owner ratifies the sale, it is valid because the plot remains.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq. It states that for a fuduli sale to be ratified, the sold item must still exist in its original form. The examples clarify: if the buyer dyes the cloth, it becomes a different item, so ratification is invalid. If a slave-girl gives birth, the child is part of the mother, so ratification includes both. If a house collapses, the land remains, so ratification is valid on the land. The key principle is that the item must be the same as when the sale was made.

Key Terms
قيام المبيع (qiyam al-mabi'): The requirement that the sold item remains in existence in its original state for the ratification of a fuduli sale.
فضولي (fuduli): A person who sells or buys property without the owner's permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Valid because the cloth is still there.
B) Invalid because the cloth has changed into a different item.
C) Valid if the owner accepts the dyeing.
D) Invalid unless the buyer compensates for the dye.

Key Takeaway
For ratification of a fuduli sale, the sold item must remain fundamentally unchanged; substantial alteration invalidates ratification.

Owner's Option in Fuduli Sale After Delivery and Perishment
Explaining the owner's option to hold either buyer or seller liable when the fuduli delivers the item and it perishes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a fuduli delivers the item and it perishes, and explain how the owner's choice to hold either party liable affects the validity of the sale.

Core Idea
The owner's choice of whom to hold liable determines whether the fuduli sale becomes valid or void.

The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq left out a key scenario: what happens if the fuduli actually delivers the sold item and it perishes before the owner ratifies? Imam Muhammad fills this gap by giving the owner an option to hold either the buyer or the fuduli liable. The validity of the sale then hinges on that choice and the nature of the fuduli's possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the owner's options
Recognize that the fuduli has delivered the item to the buyer and it perishes. The owner may hold liable whichever party he wishes: the buyer (مشتري) or the fuduli seller (بائع).
2
Analyze the effect of holding the buyer liable
If the owner holds the buyer liable, the sale becomes void (باطل). Reason: taking the value (price) is equivalent to taking the item itself, so the owner's act of holding the buyer liable is like taking the item back, invalidating the sale.
The buyer can still reclaim the price from the fuduli, but not what he paid as liability.
3
Analyze the effect of holding the seller liable
If the owner holds the fuduli liable, the sale's validity depends on the type of possession the fuduli had before delivery. If his possession was guaranteed (مضمون), the sale becomes valid because the liability establishes his ownership retrospectively. If his possession was a trust (أمانة), the sale does not become valid because the liability arises after the contract.
4
Understand the qiyas to a usurper
Imam Muhammad's reasoning compares the fuduli who delivers first, then becomes liable, then sells, to a usurper (مغصوب) who becomes owner by liability. This analogy supports the validity when possession is guaranteed.
The qiyas is zanni but accepted in Zahir al-Riwayah.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a specific case, identify which option the owner would likely choose and determine the sale's validity based on the type of possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المؤلف حكم تسليم المبيع من الفضولي فلو سلمه فهلك فللمالك أن يضمن أيهما شاء فأيهما اختار ضمانه برئ الآخر لأن في التضمين تمليكاً منه فإذا ملكه من أحدهما لا يمكن تمليكه من الآخر، فإن اختار تضمين المشتري بطل البيع لأن أخذ القيمة كأخذ العين ويرجع المشتري على البائع بالثمن لا بما ضمن، وإن اختار تضمين البائع ينظر؛ إن كان قبض البائع مضموناً عليه نفذ بيعه بالضمان لأن ملکه قد عقده، وإن كان قبضه أمانة فإنما صار مضموناً عليه بالتسليم بعد البيع فلا ينفذ بيعه تم بالضمان لتأخر سبب ملکه عن العقد . وقد ذكر محمد في ظاهر الرواية أنه يجوز البيع بتضمين البائع، ووجهه سلم أولاً ثم صار مضموناً عليه ثم باعه فصار كالمغصوب ؛ كذا في البزازية وقيد بالبيع لأنه إذا اشترى لغيره كان ما اشتراه لنفسه أجاز الذي اشتراه له أم لا .
The author did not mention the ruling on delivery of the sold item by the fuduli. If he delivers it and it perishes, the owner may hold liable whichever of them he wishes. Whichever one he chooses to hold liable, the other is released, because in holding liable there is a transfer of ownership from him; so if he transfers ownership to one, it cannot be transferred to the other. If he chooses to hold the buyer liable, the sale is void because taking the value is like taking the item itself, and the buyer can claim the price from the seller, but not what he was held liable for. If he chooses to hold the seller liable, then consider: if the seller's possession was guaranteed (mamun), his sale is executed by the liability because his ownership is established by the contract; if his possession was a trust (amanah), then it only becomes guaranteed by delivery after the sale, so his sale is not executed by the liability because the cause of his ownership is after the contract. Muhammad stated in the apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah) that the sale is valid by holding the seller liable, and its reasoning is that he delivered first, then became liable, then sold, so it becomes like a usurped item. This is in al-Bazzaziyyah. And it is restricted to sale, because if he purchases for someone else, what he purchases is for himself, whether the one for whom he purchased ratifies or not.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage expands on a missing ruling from the matn. The owner has the option to hold either the buyer (مشتري) or the fuduli seller (بائع) liable for the price if the item perishes after delivery. If the owner holds the buyer liable, the sale is void, and the buyer can claim the price from the fuduli (since they had a contract). If the owner holds the fuduli liable, the validity depends on whether the fuduli's possession was guaranteed (مضمون) or a trust (أمانة). Imam Muhammad's view in Zahir al-Riwayah allows the sale to be executed by holding the fuduli liable, analogizing to a usurper (مغصوب). However, this ruling applies only to sale, not to purchase on behalf of another.

Key Terms
تضمين (tadmin): Holding someone liable for the value of a perished item; also, a transfer of ownership by the owner.
قبض مضمون (qabd mamun): Possession that is guaranteed, meaning the possessor is liable for the item's value (e.g., a buyer's possession before payment).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the fuduli delivers the item and it perishes, and the owner chooses to hold the buyer liable, what happens to the sale?
A) The sale is valid because liability is transferred.
B) The sale is void because taking the value is like taking the item.
C) The sale becomes suspended until the buyer pays.
D) The sale is valid only if the buyer agrees.

Key Takeaway
The owner's choice of whom to hold liable in a fuduli sale determines the validity of the sale: holding the buyer voids it, while holding the seller may validate it if his possession was guaranteed.

Fuduli Purchasing: Suspension and Ratification
Explanation of fuduli purchase rulings with suspension and ratification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a fuduli adding the contract to himself versus to a third party, and state the corresponding ruling on ownership and ratification.

Core Idea
If the fuduli adds the contract to himself, he owns; if to another, it is suspended on that other's ratification.

When an unauthorized person (fuduli) buys an item on behalf of another, the wording he uses determines ownership. If he phrases the contract as for himself, ownership vests in him immediately; if he phrases it for a third party, the contract is suspended pending that party's ratification. This lesson unpacks the rules, including scenarios of disagreement and post-purchase alterations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish whether the fuduli adds the contract to himself (e.g., 'I buy this for me') or to a third party (e.g., 'I buy this for Zayd').
The wording 'اشتريت منك لكذا لفلان' adds to third party; 'اشتريت لنفسي' adds to self.
2
Determine the legal effect
If added to self, the fuduli becomes owner; ratification by the intended recipient is irrelevant. If added to third party, the contract is suspended (mawquf) on that person's ratification.
The text uses 'يتوقف على إجازة من المشتري له' for suspension.
3
Analyze ratification scenarios
Study cases: when the fuduli claims no agency, when the intended recipient ratifies after delivery, and conflicts of claim. The text gives detailed examples.
Key: if the fuduli was not an agent, ownership goes to him; later ratification does not transfer it.
4
Examine the effect of alteration on ratification
If the fuduli alters the item (dyeing, cutting, sewing) before ratification, there is a dispute: al-Bazzaziyyah says ratification invalid; Manh al-Ghaffar says valid only if item is not completely changed (cutting/sewing invalidates).
The difference hinges on whether the item is considered 'the same' after alteration.
5
Apply the framework
Given a new scenario (e.g., fuduli buys a car for Ahmad, adds contract to Ahmad, later Ahmad ratifies after fuduli repaints the car), determine the ruling using the taught distinctions.
Use the correct view (al-sahih) that suspension requires ratification; slight alteration may not prevent ratification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يجد نفاذاً يتوقف على إجازة من المشتري له كالصبي المحجور يشتري شيئاً لغيره فيتوقف هذا إذا أضاف العقد إلى نفسه، أما إذا أضافه إلى غيره بأن يقول بع هذا العبد لفلان فقال البائع بعته لفلان يتوقف على أجازته، وأما إذا قال اشتريت منك بكذا الأجل فلان فقال البائع بعت أو قال البائع بعت منك لفلان فإنه يقع الشراء للمخاطب لا لفلان والصحيح أنه إذا أضيف العقد في أحد الكلامين إلى يتوقف على إجازة فلان ولو اشترى عبداً وأشهد أنه يشتريه لفلان وقال فلان رضيت فالعقد للمشتري لأنه إذا لم يكن وكيلاً بالشراء وقع الملك له فلا اعتبار بالإجازة بعد ذلك وهي تلحق العقد الموقوف لا النافذ، فإن دفع المشتري إليه العبد وأخذ الثمن كان بيعاً بالتعاطي بينهما، ولو ظن المشتري والمشتري له أن الملك وقع للمشتري له فسلمه له بعد قبض ثمنه لا يسترد بلا رضا المشتري له ويجعل كأنه ولاه وإن علما أن الشراء وقع للمشتري بعده وإن زعم المشتري له أن الشراء كان بأمره ووقع الملك له والمشتري أنه كان بلا أمره ووقع الشراء للمشتري فالقول للمشتري له لأن الشراء بإقراره وقع له؛ كذا في البزازية. لو أجازه بعد صبغ الثوب المشتري فإنه لا يجوز كذا في البزازية، وفي منح الغفار ما يخالفه فإنه قال : والمراد بكون المبيع قائماً أن لا يكون متغيراً بحيث يعد شيئاً آخر فإنه لو باع ثوب غيره بغير أمره وصبغه المشتري فأجاز رب الثوب البيع جاز ، ولو قطعه وحاطه ثم أجاز البيع لا يجوز لأنه صار شيئاً آخر اهـ. والمسألة بهذا اللفظ دون التعليل في التتارخانية عن فتاوى أبي الليث. قوله: (والصحيح أنه إذا أضيف العقد في أحد الكلامين إلى فلان يتوقف الخ) ظاهره أنه يتوقف وإن أضيف في الكلام الآخر إلى الفضولي ويأتي قريباً أن أصح الروايتين في هذه الصورة أنه
And if it does not have effect, it is suspended on ratification by the one for whom it was purchased, like a minor under interdiction who buys something for someone else. This is when he adds the contract to himself. As for when he adds it to someone else, such as saying 'Sell this slave to so-and-so' and the seller says 'I sold it to so-and-so,' it is suspended on his ratification. If he says 'I bought from you for so-and-so at such a term' and the seller says 'I sold,' or the seller says 'I sold to you for so-and-so,' then the purchase occurs for the addressee, not for so-and-so. The correct view is that if the contract is added in one of the two statements to so-and-so, it is suspended on the ratification of so-and-so. If he buys a slave and bears witness that he is buying it for so-and-so, and so-and-so says 'I am pleased,' the contract is for the buyer because if he was not an agent in the purchase, ownership falls to him, so ratification afterwards is not considered; it attaches to the suspended contract, not the executed one. If the buyer gives the slave to him and takes the price, it is a sale by mutual transaction between them. If the buyer and the one for whom it was bought think that ownership falls to the latter, and he delivers it to him after receiving the price, he cannot take it back without the latter's consent, and it is considered as if he gave it as a gift (tawliyah). If they know that the purchase was for the buyer afterwards, and the one for whom it was bought claims that the purchase was by his order and ownership fell to him, and the buyer claims it was without his order and the purchase was for the buyer, then the word is of the one for whom it was bought because the purchase by his admission fell to him. This is in al-Bazzaziyyah. 'If he ratifies after the buyer dyed the garment, it is not valid' is also in al-Bazzaziyyah. In Manh al-Ghaffar, there is something contradicting it, for he said: 'The meaning of the sold item being existent is that it should not have changed to the extent that it is considered something else. So if he sold someone else's garment without his order, and the buyer dyed it, and the owner of the garment ratified the sale, it is valid. And if he cut it and sewed it, and then ratified the sale, it is not valid because it has become something else.' This is the issue with this wording without the reasoning in al-Tatarkhaniyyah from the fatwas of Abu al-Layth. His statement: 'And the correct view is that if the contract is added in one of the two statements to so-and-so, it is suspended...' Its apparent meaning is that it is suspended even if it is added in the other statement to the fuduli, and it will come shortly that the soundest of the two narrations in this form is that...
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings on a fuduli buying for another. The key distinction is whether the fuduli adds the contract to himself or to the third party. If he adds to himself, the ownership is his unless he was an agent; later ratification does not change that. If he adds to the third party in the statement, the contract is suspended on the third party's ratification. Various scenarios are examined, including when the buyer and the intended recipient disagree. The passage also revisits the dyeing issue with a conflicting view from Manh al-Ghaffar, stating that dyeing does not invalidate ratification if the item is still considered the same (unlike cutting and sewing). The matn comment indicates that the correct view is that the contract is suspended when added to a third party in one statement.

Key Terms
إضافة العقد (idafat al-'aqd): Adding the contract to a specific party in the wording, which determines to whom ownership first falls.
محجور (mahjur): A person under legal interdiction (e.g., a minor) who cannot independently effect contracts.
فضولي (fuduli): A person who acts in a contract without prior authorization (unauthorized agent).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A fuduli, without being an agent, says to a seller 'I buy this car for you for $10,000' (khitab to the seller?) Actually 'I buy this car for Zayd' and the seller says 'I sell.' Which ruling applies?
A) The car belongs to the fuduli because he made the offer.
B) The car belongs to the seller because the fuduli had no authority.
C) The contract is suspended on Zayd's ratification.
D) The contract is void because the fuduli did not specify the price.

Key Takeaway
The fuduli's wording determines ownership; adding to self gives immediate ownership, adding to another requires ratification.

8 min
The Four Types of Fuduli Purchase
Categorization of fuduli purchase into four types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four types of fuduli purchase by their wording and state the ruling for each type.

Core Idea
The validity of a fuduli purchase depends on whether the contract is added to the third party in both statements.

The fuqaha have categorized fuduli purchases based on the wording used by the seller and the fuduli. These categories determine whether the contract is suspended, executed, or void. Let us examine the four types as laid out by al-Karabisi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the speaker and addressee
In each type, note who is speaking and to whom the speech is addressed. This determines the category.
2
Classify the first type
When seller says 'I sold this to Zaid' and fuduli says 'I bought for Zaid' or 'I accepted' (without 'for Zaid'), the contract is suspended.
The contract is added to Zaid in seller's statement but not in buyer's.
3
Classify the second type
When seller says 'I sold from Zaid' and buyer says 'I bought for his sake' or 'I accepted', it is suspended.
Both statements refer to Zaid.
4
Classify the third type
When seller says 'I sold this to you' and buyer says 'I bought' intending it for someone else, it is executed on the buyer himself.
The seller addressed the buyer, so the contract is valid for the buyer.
5
Classify the fourth type
When buyer says 'I bought for Zaid' and seller says 'I sold to you', the contract is void according to the soundest narration, because the buyer's statement does not respond to the seller's.
The miss-match voids the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فروق الكرابيسي : شراء الفضولي على أربعة أوجه الأول أن يقول البائع بعت هذا لفلان بكذا والفضولي يقول اشتريت لفلان بكذا أو قبلت ولم يقل لفلان فهذا يتوقف. الثاني أن يقول البائع بعت من فلان بكذا والمشتري يقول اشتريته لأجله أو قبلت يتوقف. الثالث أن يقول البائع بعت هذا منك بكذا فقال اشتريت أو قبلت ونوى أن يكون لفلان فإنه ينفذ على المشتري. الرابع لو قال اشتريت لفلان بكذا والبائع يقول بعت منك بطل العقد في أصح الروايتين. والفرق أنه خاطب المشتري والمشتري يسترد لغيره فلا يكون جواباً فكان شرط العقد بخلاف الفصلين الأولين إذا العقد أضيف إلى فلان في الكلامين، وبخلاف الفصل الثالث لأنه وجد نفاذاً على العاقد وقد أضيف العقد إليه .اهـ. وأشار المؤلف بثبوت الفسخ والإجازة للمالك إلى أن الفضولي لو شرط الخيار للمالك فإن العقد يبطل ولا يتوقف لأن الخيار له بدون الشرط فيكون الشرط له مبطلاً؛ كذا في فروق الكرابيسي. وقيد ببيع ملك الغير لأنه لو باع ملك نفسه مشغولاً بحق الغير كالرهن إذا باعه الراهن والعين المؤجرة إذا باعها المؤجر يتوقف العقد على إجازة المرتهن والمستأجر فيملكانها دون الفسخ على الصحيح كما سيأتي وفرق بينهما الكرابيسي فجعل للمرتهن الإجازة والفسخ دون المستأجر فلا يملكه فارقاً بأن المستأجر حقه في المنفعة، ولذا لو هلكت العين لا يسقط دينه وفي الرهن يسقط وهو استيفاء حكمي. وتفرع على الفرق ما لو تعدد بيع المؤجر فأجاز المستأجر الثاني نفذ الأول، ولو تعدد بيع الرهن فأجاز المرتهن الثاني نفذ لا الأول اهـ. ولو الله تعالى باع ملك غيره لمالكه لكان أولى لأنه لو باعه لنفسه لم ينعقد أصلاً كما في البدائع. ولا بد أن يقول بغير إذنه ليكون فضولياً ولو تعدد تصرف الفضولي كأمة
In Furq al-Karabisi: The purchase of a fuduli is of four types: First: the seller says 'I sold this to so-and-so for such amount' and the fuduli says 'I bought for so-and-so for such amount' or 'I accepted' and did not say 'for so-and-so' – this is suspended. Second: the seller says 'I sold from so-and-so for such amount' and the buyer says 'I bought it for his sake' or 'I accepted' – it is suspended. Third: the seller says 'I sold this to you for such amount' and he says 'I bought' or 'I accepted' and intended it to be for so-and-so – it is executed on the buyer. Fourth: if he said 'I bought for so-and-so for such amount' and the seller says 'I sold to you' – the contract is void according to the soundest of the two narrations. The difference: he addressed the buyer and the buyer is acting for someone else, so it is not a response, so it was a condition of the contract, unlike the first two chapters because the contract was added to so-and-so in both statements, and unlike the third chapter because execution was found on the contracting party and the contract was added to him. End. And the author indicated by establishing the right of rescission and ratification for the owner that if the fuduli stipulates an option for the owner, the contract becomes void and is not suspended, because the owner already has the option without the condition, so the condition is invalidating for him. This is in Furq al-Karabisi. And it is restricted to sale of another's property, because if he sells his own property encumbered with a right of another, such as a mortgage if the mortgagor sells it, or a leased property if the lessor sells it, the contract is suspended on the ratification of the mortgagee and lessee, and they own it without the right of rescission according to the correct view, as will come. Al-Karabisi distinguished between them, making the mortgagee have the right of ratification and rescission, while the lessee does not have the right of rescission, because the lessee's right is in the usufruct. Hence, if the property perishes, his debt does not fall, whereas in mortgage it falls, and this is a formal fulfillment. From this difference arises: if there are multiple sales by the lessor, and the second lessee ratifies, the first sale is executed; if there are multiple sales of the mortgage, and the second mortgagee ratifies, the first is not executed. End. And if Allah – exalted – sells another's property to its owner, it is more appropriate, because if he sells it to himself, it does not contract at all, as in al-Bada'i'. And it is necessary that he says 'without his permission' to be a fuduli. And if the actions of the fuduli are multiple, like a slave-girl...
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage from Furq al-Karabisi categorizes fuduli purchases into four types based on the wording used. Types 1 and 2 are suspended on ratification; type 3 is executed on the fuduli himself; type 4 is void. The key is whether the contract is added to the third party and whether the response matches. Then it discusses ruling on stipulating an option for the owner: such a condition is invalid and voids the contract because the owner already has the right of rescission. Finally, it distinguishes between fuduli sale (selling another's property without permission) and selling one's own property that is encumbered (e.g., mortgaged or leased). In the latter, the contract is suspended on the right-holder's ratification, but they do not have the right of rescission (except mortgagee). The passage ends with a note on multiple transactions.

Key Terms
الخيار (al-khiyar): The option to rescind or ratify a contract; here specifically the owner's inherent option in a fuduli sale.
مشغول بحق الغير (mashghul bihaqq al-ghayr): Property that is encumbered with a right of another, such as a mortgage or lease.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Furq al-Karabisi, which type of fuduli purchase is executed on the fuduli himself?
A) Type 1
B) Type 2
C) Type 3
D) Type 4

Key Takeaway
The wording of the contract in fuduli purchase determines whether it is suspended, executed, or void.

6 min
Stipulating an Option for the Owner in Fuduli Sale
Ruling on stipulating option for owner in fuduli sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why stipulating an option for the owner in a fuduli sale makes the contract void and not suspended.

Core Idea
The owner already has the option to rescind, so stipulating it is a futile condition that invalidates the contract.

In a fuduli sale, the owner of the property has the inherent right to ratify or rescind the contract. If the fuduli makes this right a condition in the contract, the condition is superfluous and voids the contract entirely, as it adds a condition that has no legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the owner's inherent right
In a fuduli sale, the owner has the option (khiyar) to ratify or rescind the contract without any condition.
This is a default right, not a stipulated one.
2
Recognize the effect of stipulating a condition
If the fuduli stipulates in the contract that the owner has the option, this condition is unnecessary because the right already exists.
Such a condition is considered futile (laghw).
3
Understand the consequence on the contract
Adding a futile condition makes the contract void (batil), not merely suspended, because it introduces an invalid condition.
The contract is void ab initio, not awaiting ratification.

Selling Encumbered Property vs. Fuduli Sale
Distinction between fuduli sale and sale of encumbered property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a fuduli sale (selling another's property) and selling one's own property encumbered with a right of another (e.g., mortgage or lease), and identify the different rulings on ratification and rescission.

Core Idea
Selling encumbered property is not fuduli; the contract is suspended on the right-holder's ratification, but the right-holder does not have the right to rescind (except mortgagee).

The previous lessons dealt with selling another's property (fuduli). Now we consider a different scenario: a person sells his own property that is encumbered with someone else's right, such as a mortgage or lease. The rulings differ: the contract is suspended on ratification of the mortgagee or lessee, but their rights regarding rescission are not the same.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of sale
Determine whether the seller is selling another's property (fuduli) or his own property that is encumbered (e.g., mortgaged or leased).
The text restricts the fuduli ruling to 'selling another's property'.
2
Understand the ruling for encumbered property
If a person sells his own mortgaged property, the sale is suspended on the mortgagee's ratification. The mortgagee has both the right to ratify and to rescind.
This is according to al-Karabisi's distinction.
3
Contrast with leased property
If a lessor sells the leased property, the sale is also suspended on the lessee's ratification, but the lessee does not have the right to rescind. The lessee can only ratify or let the sale proceed.
The difference is due to the nature of their right: the lessee's right is in usufruct, not the property itself.
4
Apply the difference to multiple transactions
If there are multiple sales (e.g., lessor sells to two buyers), if the second lessee ratifies, the first sale is executed. In mortgage, if the second mortgagee ratifies, the first is not executed.
This shows the practical effect of the distinction.

According to al-Karabisi, what is the difference between a mortgagee and a lessee when ratifying a sale of encumbered property?

intermediate
10 min
Four Types of Purchase by an Unauthorized Agent and the Effect of Stipulating a Condition
Lists four types of fuḍūlī purchase and addresses stipulating a condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four categories of a fuḍūlī's purchase and explain why stipulating an option for the owner invalidates the contract.

Core Idea
A fuḍūlī's purchase is suspended; stipulating an option for the owner renders the contract void.

The contract made by an unauthorized agent (fuḍūlī) does not automatically bind the owner. Instead, it is suspended (mawqūf) until the owner ratifies it. This lesson examines four distinct scenarios based on how the offer and acceptance are phrased, and then explores what happens if the fuḍūlī adds a condition granting the owner an option (khiyār). Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying Hanafi contract law.

Guided Path
1
Define the fuḍūlī and the concept of suspension
Start by clarifying that a fuḍūlī acts without prior permission. His contract is not immediately binding; it is suspended (mawqūf) pending ratification by the owner. This suspension is a core Hanafi doctrine.
Suspension means the contract is neither valid nor void until the owner agrees.
2
Identify the four scenarios from Furūq al-Karābīsī
List the four cases: (1) seller addresses the principal, buyer replies but does not mention principal; (2) seller mentions principal, buyer agrees on his behalf; (3) seller addresses the fuḍūlī, who intends for another; (4) fuḍūlī says 'I bought for X' but seller says 'I sold to you'. Note the wording difference in each.
The fourth scenario is void in the stronger narration.
3
Determine the ruling for each scenario
Cases 1 and 2 are suspended; case 3 is effective against the fuḍūlī himself; case 4 is void. Understand the reasoning: suspension arises when the contract is attributed to the principal in both statements or when the reply is addressed to the fuḍūlī but he intends for another.
Case 3: the fuḍūlī becomes the actual purchaser.
4
Analyze the effect of stipulating a condition for the owner
If the fuḍūlī adds a condition like 'on condition that the owner has the option for three days', the contract becomes invalid (bāṭil), not suspended. This is because the owner already has the inherent right to ratify or reject; stipulating it explicitly is meaningless and invalidates the contract.
The condition is considered superfluous and harmful.
5
Contrast with selling encumbered property
The ruling differs if the seller is the owner but the property is encumbered (e.g., mortgaged or leased). In such cases, the contract is suspended upon the mortgagee's or lessee's ratification, but the mortgagee has the right to rescind while the lessee does not.
This distinction is based on the nature of the right: a mortgagee's right is in the corpus, a lessee's right is in usufruct.

Source Text
