وفي فروق الكرابيسي : شراء الفضولي على أربعة أوجه الأول أن يقول البائع بعت هذا لفلان بكذا والفضولي يقول اشتريت لفلان بكذا أو قبلت ولم يقل لفلان فهذا يتوقف. الثاني أن يقول البائع بعت من فلان بكذا والمشتري يقول اشتريته لأجله أو قبلت يتوقف. الثالث أن يقول البائع بعت هذا منك بكذا فقال اشتريت أو قبلت ونوى أن يكون لفلان فإنه ينفذ على المشتري. الرابع لو قال اشتريت لفلان بكذا والبائع يقول بعت منك بطل العقد في أصح الروايتين. والفرق أنه خاطب المشتري والمشتري يسترد لغيره فلا يكون جواباً فكان شرط العقد بخلاف الفصلين الأولين إذا العقد أضيف إلى فلان في الكلامين، وبخلاف الفصل الثالث لأنه وجد نفاذاً على العاقد وقد أضيف العقد إليه .اهـ. وأشار المؤلف بثبوت الفسخ والإجازة للمالك إلى أن الفضولي لو شرط الخيار للمالك فإن العقد يبطل ولا يتوقف لأن الخيار له بدون الشرط فيكون الشرط له مبطلاً؛ كذا في فروق الكرابيسي. وقيد ببيع ملك الغيره لأنه لو باع ملك نفسه مشغولاً بحق الغير كالرهن إذا باعه الراهن والعين المؤجرة إذا باعها المؤجر يتوقف العقد على إجازة المرتهن والمستأجر فيملكانها دون الفسخ على الصحيح كما سيأتي وفرق بينهما الكرابيسي فجعل للمرتهن الإجازة والفسخ دون المستأجر فلا يملكه فارقاً بأن المستأجر حقه في المنفعة، ولذا لو هلكت العين لا يسقط دينه وفي الرهن يسقط وهو استيفاء حكمي. وتفرع على الفرق ما لو تعدد بيع المؤجر فأجاز المستأجر الثاني نفذ الأول، ولو تعدد بيع الرهن فأجاز المرتهن الثاني نفذ لا الأول اهـ. ولو الله تعالى باع ملك غيره لمالكه لكان أولى لأنه لو باعه لنفسه لم ينعقد أصلاً كما في البدائع. ولا بد أن يقول بغير إذنه ليكون فضولياً ولو تعدد تصرف الفضولي كأمة قال المصنف رحمه يبطل. قوله: (وفي فروق الكرابيسي شراء الفضولي على أربعة أوجه قال في البزازية : قال بعت لفلان وقال المشتري اشتريت أو قبلت لفلان أو لم يقل لفلان أو قال الفضولين ! بع لفلان فقال بعت وقال اشتريت لفلان توقف، ولو قال بعت منك فقال الفضولي اشتريت أو قبلت ونوى بقلبه لفلان لا يتوقف، أو قال الفضولي اشتريت لفلان وقال البائع بعت منك الأصح عدم التوقف، ولو قال بعت هذا منك لفلان فقال المشتري اشتريت أو قبلت أو قال المشتري اشتريت لأجل فلان وقال البائع بعت لا يتوقف وينفذ اتفاقاً، ولو قال الفضولي اشتريت لفلان على أنه بالخيار ثلاثاً لا يتوقف بخلاف شرائه لفلان بلا خيار اهـ من التاسع في الوكالة بالشراء وفيه .الفضولي وفي الخانية بعد قوله «لا يتوقف» : وإنما يتوقف شراء الفضولي إذا اشترى بغير خيار. قوله : (بطل العقد في أصح الروايتين) وعلى هذا فالاكتفاء بالإضافة في أحد الكلامين بأن لا يضاف إلى الآخر نهر أي الاكتفاء بالإضافة إلى فلان على ما مر تصحيح مصور بأن لا يضاف إلى المشتري بأن يقول البائع بعت ولا يقول منك، فإذا أضيف لا يتوقف، وإن زاد على ذلك لفلان لا يتوقف أيضاً لكنه ينفذ كما قدمناه . عن البزازية . قوله : (فيكون الشرط له مبطلاً) قال في النهر : كان ينبغي أن يكون الشرط لغواً فقط فتدبره.
In Furūq al-Karābīsī: The purchase of the unauthorized agent (fuḍūlī) is on four types: First, that the seller says 'I sold this to so-and-so for such amount' and the fuḍūlī says 'I bought for so-and-so for such amount' or 'I accepted' and did not say 'for so-and-so' — this is suspended. Second, that the seller says 'I sold from so-and-so for such amount' and the buyer says 'I bought it on his behalf' or 'I accepted' — suspended. Third, that the seller says 'I sold this from you for such amount' and he said 'I bought' or 'I accepted' and intended it to be for so-and-so — it is effective against the buyer. Fourth, if he said 'I bought for so-and-so for such amount' and the seller says 'I sold from you' — the contract is void in the more authentic of the two narrations. The difference is that he addressed the buyer and the buyer is rejecting on behalf of another, so it is not a reply, thus it is a condition of the contract contrary to the first two cases where the contract is attributed to so-and-so in both statements, and contrary to the third case because it is effective on the contracting party and the contract has been attributed to him. End. And the author indicated by establishing the right of rescission and ratification for the owner that if the fuḍūlī stipulates a condition (option) for the owner, the contract becomes void and is not suspended, because the option belongs to him without the stipulation, so stipulating for him is invalidating; thus in Furūq al-Karābīsī. And he restricted it to selling the property of another because if he sold his own property that is encumbered by a right of another, like collateral when the debtor sells it, and a leased property when the lessor sells it, the contract is suspended upon the ratification of the mortgagee and lessee, and they have the right to own it without rescission according to the sound view, as will come, and al-Karābīsī differentiated between them, giving the mortgagee the right of ratification and rescission but not the lessee — he does not possess it, distinguishing by the fact that the lessee's right is in the usufruct, therefore if the property perishes his debt does not fall, while in collateral it falls, and it is a constructive satisfaction. And branching from this difference: if the lessor makes multiple sales and the second lessee ratifies, the first takes effect; if there are multiple sales of collateral and the second mortgagee ratifies, the first does not take effect. End. And if someone sells the property of another to its owner, it would be more appropriate, because if he sells it to himself it is not concluded at all, as in al-Badā’i‘. And it is necessary that he say 'without his permission' to be a fuḍūlī. And if the actions of the fuḍūlī are multiple, like a female slave — the author said, may Allah have mercy on him, it becomes void. His statement: (And in Furūq al-Karābīsī: purchase of the fuḍūlī on four aspects.) He said in al-Bazzāziyyah: He said 'I sold to so-and-so' and the buyer said 'I bought' or 'I accepted' for so-and-so or did not say 'for so-and-so', or the two fuḍūlī! 'Sell to so-and-so' and he said 'I sold' and he said 'I bought for so-and-so' — suspended. If he said 'I sold from you' and the fuḍūlī said 'I bought' or 'I accepted' and intended in his heart for so-and-so — not suspended. Or if the fuḍūlī said 'I bought for so-and-so' and the seller said 'I sold from you' — the correct view is non-suspension. And if he said 'I sold this from you for so-and-so' and the buyer said 'I bought' or 'I accepted', or the buyer said 'I bought on behalf of so-and-so' and the seller said 'I sold' — not suspended and effective unanimously. And if the fuḍūlī said 'I bought for so-and-so on condition that he has the option for three days' — not suspended, unlike his purchase for so-and-so without option. End from the ninth section on agency to purchase, and in it 'the fuḍūlī'. And in al-Khāniyyah after his statement 'not suspended': Purchase of the fuḍūlī is only suspended if he buys without option. His statement: (The contract is void in the more authentic of the two narrations.) And upon this, sufficiency of attribution in one of the two statements by not attributing to the other — i.e., sufficiency of attribution to so-and-so as has passed, clarifying by way of example: that he does not attribute to the buyer by the seller saying 'I sold' and not saying 'from you'. When he attributes, it is not suspended, and if he adds 'for so-and-so', it is also not suspended but is effective as we have presented. From al-Bazzāziyyah. His statement: (Thus the condition for him is invalidating.) He said in al-Nahr: It should have been that the condition is merely void, so consider it.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqā’iq discusses the types of purchase by a fuḍūlī (unauthorized agent). It first cites Furūq al-Karābīsī, listing four scenarios: (1) seller says 'I sold to so-and-so' and fuḍūlī says 'I bought' or 'I accepted' without mentioning the principal — the contract is suspended (mawqūf). (2) seller says 'I sold from so-and-so' and fuḍūlī says 'I bought for him' or 'I accepted' — also suspended. (3) seller says 'I sold from you' (addressing the fuḍūlī) and fuḍūlī buys intending for another — the contract is effective against the fuḍūlī himself, not the principal. (4) fuḍūlī says 'I bought for so-and-so' and seller says 'I sold from you' — the contract is void according to the stronger narration. The reason for the difference is the direction of address and attribution. The passage then discusses the effect of stipulating a khiyār (option) for the owner: if the fuḍūlī stipulates an option for the owner, the contract becomes invalid because the owner already has the right to ratify or reject; stipulating it explicitly is meaningless and voids the contract. The author then distinguishes between selling property of another and selling one's own property encumbered by a right (like mortgage or lease). In the latter, the contract is suspended on the ratification of the mortgagee or lessee, but the difference is that the mortgagee has the right to rescind while the lessee does not. This is because the lessee's right is only in usufruct, while the mortgagee's right is in the corpus. Finally, the passage includes additional commentary from al-Bazzāziyyah and al-Nahr, clarifying the details of the four categories and the ruling on stipulating a condition.

Key Terms
فضولي (fuḍūlī): An unauthorized person who acts on behalf of another without prior permission, such as buying or selling property of another.
خيار (khiyār): An option to rescind or confirm a contract; here, the owner's right to ratify or reject the fuḍūlī's contract.
توقف (tawaqquf): The state of being suspended or conditional; the contract does not take effect until ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fourth scenario of fuḍūlī purchase, the contract is void because:
A) The seller did not consent to the buyer.
B) The buyer addressed the seller on behalf of another, but the seller's reply was addressed to the buyer personally.
C) The buyer did not mention the price.
D) The seller refused to sell to the principal.

Key Takeaway
A fuḍūlī's contract is suspended (mawqūf) unless a condition is stipulated for the owner, in which case it becomes void.

Priority of Contracts by a Fuḍūlī
Rules for multiple transactions by a fuḍūlī

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi hierarchy of contract strength and apply it to determine which transaction prevails when a fuḍūlī executes multiple conflicting contracts.

Core Idea
When multiple contracts conflict, the strongest prevails: emancipation > sale > lease > mortgage > gift.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq expands on the rulings of a fuḍūlī who engages in multiple concurrent transactions affecting the same property. The key question is: if two or more contracts conflict and are ratified, which one takes effect? The Hanafi school answers by ranking contracts according to their legal strength—based on binding nature and permanence. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for resolving real-world disputes over unauthorized dealings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting transactions
List all the pairs of transactions mentioned in the text (e.g., sale vs marriage, sale vs lease, sale vs gift, etc.). Note that each pair involves the same item and both are ratified.
The fuḍūlī performs multiple acts; the owner may ratify some or all.
2
Recall the hierarchy of strength
From the text, extract the ranking: emancipation (itq), kitāba, and tadbīr are strongest because they are binding (lāzim). Sale is stronger than lease and gift. Lease is stronger than mortgage. Gift with possession can equal sale for indivisible property.
The text explicitly states: 'البيع أقوى' (sale is stronger) and 'الإجارة أحق من الرهن' (lease is more entitled than mortgage).
3
Apply the hierarchy to each case
For each pair, determine the stronger contract using the hierarchy. For example, sale vs marriage: sale prevails because it is a permanent transfer of ownership, while marriage is a temporary relationship subject to dissolution.
Special case: two sales by different fuḍūlīs result in equal division because both are of equal strength.
4
Handle exceptions and equal-strength cases
Note that gift with possession (qabḍ) in an indivisible property equals sale, so each party takes half. Also, two marriages to different men by a fuḍūlī are both void because marriage cannot be shared.
The text also covers usurper (ghāṣib) scenarios, but these follow different principles.

Source Text

باعها فضولي من رجل وزوجها منه آخر فأجيزا معاً يثبت الأقوى فتصير مملوكة لا زوجة . ولو زوجاها كل من رجل فأجيز بطلاً، ولو باعها كل من رجل فأجيزا تتنصف بينهما ويخير كل منهما بين أخذ النصف أو الترك. ولو باعه فضولي وأجره آخر أو رهنه أو زوجه فأجيزا معاً ثبت الأقوى فيجوز البيع ويبطل غيره لأن البيع أقوى، وكذا تثبت الهبة إذا وهبه فضولي وآجره آخر. وكل من العتق والكتابة والتدبير أحق من غيرها لأنها لازمة بخلاف غيرها، والإجارة أحق من الرهن لإفادتها ملك المنفعة بخلاف الرهن والبيع أحق من الهبة لأن الهبة تبطل بالشيوع ففيما لا تبطل بالشيوع كهبة فضولي عبداً وبيع آخر إياه يستويان لأن الهبة مع القبض تساوي البيع في إفادة الملك وهبة المشاع فيما لا يقسم صحيحة فيأخذ كل النصف، ولو تبايع غاصباً عرضي لرجل واحد فأجاز المالك لم يجز لأن فائدة البيع بثبوت الملك في الرقبة والتصرف وهما حاصلان للمالك في البدلين بدون هذا العقد فلم ينعقد فلم يلحقه إجازة. ولو غصبا من رجلين وتبايعا وأجاز المالك جاز. ولو غصبا النقدين من واحد وعقدا
If a fuḍūlī sells her (a female slave) to a man and marries her to another, and both are ratified simultaneously, the stronger (contract) is established, so she becomes a property (owned) not a wife. If each of two men marries her (separately) and both are ratified, (both) become void. If each of two men sells her (separately) and both are ratified, she is divided equally between them, and each is given the choice of taking half or leaving. If a fuḍūlī sells it and another leases it, or mortgages it, or marries it, and both are ratified simultaneously, the stronger is established, so the sale becomes valid and the others are void because sale is stronger. Similarly, a gift (hiba) is established if a fuḍūlī gives it as a gift and another leases it. And each of emancipation (itq), kitāba, and tadbīr is more entitled than others because they are binding (lāzim), unlike others. Leasing (ijāra) is more entitled than mortgage (rahn) because it confers ownership of usufruct, unlike mortgage. Sale is more entitled than gift because gift is annulled by common ownership (shuyū‘); so in what is not annulled by common ownership, like a fuḍūlī gifting a slave and another selling it, they are equal because gift with possession equals sale in conferring ownership, and the gift of a common share in what is indivisible is valid, so each takes half. If two usurpers (ghāṣibān) of goods exchange them with one person and the owner ratifies, it is not valid because the benefit of the sale is establishing ownership in the corpus and disposal, and these are already obtained by the owner in the two compensations without this contract, so it is not concluded and ratification does not affect it. If two usurpers from two persons exchange and the owners ratify, it is valid. If two usurpers of coins (nuqūd) from one person and they contract...
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage details the rulings when a fuḍūlī (unauthorized agent) performs multiple concurrent transactions on the same item, such as selling and marrying a female slave, or selling and leasing, etc. The principle is that when two contracts conflict and both are ratified, the stronger contract prevails. The hierarchy of strength is: emancipation (itq), kitāba (contract of manumission), tadbīr (postponed manumission) are strongest because they are binding. Then lease (ijāra) is stronger than mortgage (rahn) because lease grants ownership of usufruct. Sale (bay‘) is stronger than gift (hiba) because gift can be annulled by common ownership; however, if the gift is accompanied by possession (qabḍ) and the item is indivisible, they are equal. The passage also covers cases with two sales, two marriages, and the scenario of usurpers exchanging goods: if the same owner's goods are exchanged with a third party, ratification doesn't help because the owner already owns the proceeds; but if the goods belong to two different owners, ratification makes the exchange valid.

Key Terms
فضولي (fuḍūlī): Unauthorized agent acting without prior permission.
أقوى (aqwā): Legally stronger or more effective contract that prevails over others in case of conflict.
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave; considered the strongest transaction because it is binding and cannot be revoked.
كتابة (kitāba): Contract of manumission where a slave purchases his freedom in installments; binding on both parties.
تدبير (tadbīr): Postponed manumission effective upon the owner's death; also binding.
إجارة (ijāra): Lease conveying ownership of usufruct; stronger than mortgage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A fuḍūlī sells a slave to A and gives the same slave as a gift to B, with both contracts ratified. B took possession. The slave is indivisible. What is the outcome?
A) A owns the entire slave because sale is stronger.
B) B owns the entire slave because gift with possession is stronger.
C) A and B each own half.
D) Both contracts are void.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi hierarchy of contract strength resolves conflicts between multiple fuḍūlī transactions: emancipation > sale > lease > mortgage > gift, with gift equalling sale when accompanied by possession of an indivisible item.

Mortgagee vs Lessee: Right to Annul a Suspended Sale (Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mortgagee's right to rescind

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether the mortgagee has the right to rescind a suspended sale and evaluate the intra-madhhab tarjih.

Core Idea
The mortgagee may rescind a sale of collateral without permission; the lessee cannot—difference lies in right over corpus vs usufruct.

This passage delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: does the mortgagee (murtahin) have the right to rescind a sale of the mortgaged property made without his permission? The lessee (musta'jir) clearly has no such right. The disagreement among Hanafi masters turns on the nature of the mortgagee's interest—ownership of the corpus (raqaba) versus mere usufruct. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply Hanafi principles to real transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their rights
First, distinguish between mortgagee (creditor with a right over the corpus) and lessee (right only to usufruct). This difference is key.
The mortgagee holds the collateral as security; the lessee only uses the property.
2
State the agreed ruling for the lessee
The passage affirms without dispute that the lessee cannot rescind a sale of the leased property. He can only ratify or, if the sale disturbs his tenancy, claim water.
3
Identify the dispute on the mortgagee
Two conflicting narrations: one (attributed to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) gives the mortgagee the right to rescind; the other (Abu Yusuf and some later mashā’ikh) denies it.
Both are considered within Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah by different sources.
4
Trace the tarjīḥ process
Later scholars weighed the narrations. Some (Shaykh Qāsim, al-Walwālijiyyah) adopted the view giving the right; al-Zayla‘ī held the opposite as more authentic.
The author of al-Bahr leaves it as ongoing ikhtilāf.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilāf
If the mortgagee has the right, he can independently annul the sale; if not, he can only ratify or reject the sale, but cannot actively rescind.
This affects how transactions involving collateral are managed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وفرق بينهما الكرابيسي (الخ جزم به في الخانية في فصل البيع الموقوف. وفي الفتح : وليس للمستأجر فسخ البيع بلا خلاف ولا للراهن والمؤجر، وفي المرتهن اختلاف المشايخ، وذكر قبله أن للمشتري خيار الفسخ إن لم يعلم وقت البيع بالإجارة ،والرهن وإن علم فكذلك عند محمد، قيل وهو ظاهر الرواية، وعند أبي يوسف لا ، وقيل هو ظاهر الرواية .اهـ. وفي تصحيح الشيخ قاسم أن المشايخ أخذوا بهذه الرواية اهـ . لكن ذكر في جامع الفصولين أن الأول قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد وأنه ظاهر الرواية، وفي حاشيته للرملي عن الغزي أنه هو الصحيح وعليه الفتوى كما في الولوالجية. ونقل الرملي فيها منية المفتي أنه الأصح، وفيها عن الزيلعي أن المرتهن ليس له الفسخ في أصح الروايتين. وفي جامع الفصولين عن الخانية: لو لم يجز المستأجر حتى انفسخت الإجارة نفذ البيع السابق، وكذا المرتهن إذا قضى دينه. وفيه عن الذخيرة : البيع بلا إذن المستأجر نفذ في حق البائع والمشتري لا في حق المستأجر ، فلو سقط حق المستأجر عمل ذلك البيع ولا حاجة إلى التجديد وهو الصحيح. ولو أجازه المستأجر نفذ في حق الكل ولا ينزع من يده ليصل إليه ماله إذ رضاه بالبيع يعتبر لفسخ الإجارة لا للانتزاع من يده وعن بعض بعضنا أنه لو باع وسلم وأجازهما المستأجر بطل حق حبسه، ولو أجاز البيع لا التسليم لا يبطل حق حبسه اهـ. قوله : (الثاني) مفعول أجاز وهو صفة لمحذوف أي أجاز البيع الثاني قوله: (ولو قال المصنف باع ملك غيره لمالكه لكان أولى) أي لأجل مالكه . قال الرملي : لم يذكر أحد من مشايخ المذهب الواضعين للمتون هذا القيد وأقول : تركه متعين يدل عليه توقف بيع الغاصب كما صرحوا به من غير قيد، وكما صرحوا به في الاستحقاق أن استحقاق المبيع يوجب توقف العقد على الإجازة لا نقضه في ظاهر الرواية، والظاهر أن ما قاله في البدائع رواية خارجة عن ظاهر الرواية فتأمل وارجع إلى فروع ذكرت في المحلين المذكورين يظهر
His statement: (And al-Karābīsī differentiated between them) etc. This is affirmed in al-Khāniyyah in the chapter on suspended sale. And in al-Fatḥ: The lessee has no right to rescind the sale without dispute, nor does the pledgor (rāhin) and lessor; regarding the mortgagee, there is ikhtilāf among the mashā’ikh. And he mentioned before that the buyer has the option to rescind if he did not know at the time of sale about the lease or mortgage; if he knew, then likewise according to Muhammad—it is said this is the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah—and according to Abū Yūsuf, no, and it is said this is the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah. End. And in the correction of Shaykh Qāsim, the mashā’ikh adopted this narration. End. But it is mentioned in Jāmi‘ al-Fuṣūlayn that the first is the opinion of Abū Ḥanīfah and Muḥammad and that it is Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah, and in its ḥāshiyah by al-Ramlī from al-Ghazzī that it is the ṣaḥīḥ (correct) and upon it is the fatwā as in al-Walwālijiyyah. And al-Ramlī transmitted in it from Munyat al-Muftī that it is the aṣaḥḥ (most correct), and in it from al-Zayla‘ī that the mortgagee does not have the right of rescission in the more authentic of the two narrations. And in Jāmi‘ al-Fuṣūlayn from al-Khāniyyah: If the lessee did not ratify until the lease terminated, the prior sale takes effect; similarly, if the mortgagee pays off the debt. And in it from al-Dhakhīrah: The sale without the lessee's permission takes effect vis-à-vis the seller and buyer, not vis-à-vis the lessee; so if the lessee's right falls, that sale operates and no need for renewal, and this is the ṣaḥīḥ. And if the lessee ratifies it, it takes effect vis-à-vis all, and he is not evicted from possession to receive his property because his satisfaction with the sale is considered to annul the lease, not to evict him from possession. And from some of our scholars: if he sold and delivered, and the lessee ratified both, his right of retention (ḥabs) becomes void; if he ratified the sale but not the delivery, his right of retention does not become void. End. His statement: (the second) is the object of 'ratified' and is an adjective for an omitted noun, i.e., he ratified the second sale. His statement: (And if the author said 'sells the property of another to its owner', it would be more appropriate) i.e., for its owner. Al-Ramlī said: None of the madhhab scholars who composed the matns mentioned this restriction; and I say: leaving it is determined, as indicated by the suspension of the sale of a usurper as they explicitly stated without restriction, and as they stated in the case of istiḥqāq (claim of ownership) that the istiḥqāq of the sold item necessitates suspension of the contract upon ratification, not its nullification, according to the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah. And apparently what he said in al-Badā’i‘ is a narration outside the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah, so contemplate and refer to subsidiary issues mentioned in the two places to manifest...
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage resolves an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the mortgagee (murtahin) has the right to rescind (faskh) a sale made by the mortgagor without his permission, similar to the lessee's lack of right. The text first cites al-Khāniyyah and al-Fatḥ, stating that the lessee has no right to rescind, but for the mortgagee there is ikhtilāf. It then reports that according to Muhammad (with some saying it is Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah), the buyer has the option to rescind if unaware; Abu Yusuf disagrees. Later scholars (mashā’ikh) adopted the narration giving the right. However, some sources like Jāmi‘ al-Fuṣūlayn state that the first view (no right) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad and is Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah. Al-Ramlī and others consider the correct view to be that the mortgagee does have the right of rescission, but al-Zayla‘ī says the more authentic narration is that he does not. The passage also discusses subsidiary rulings: if the lessee does not ratify until the lease ends, the sale becomes effective; similarly, if the mortgagee pays the debt. It also clarifies that the lessee's ratification is considered as annulling the lease, not as evicting himself. The final commentary by al-Ramlī addresses a point from al-Badā’i‘ about the propriety of adding a restriction in the matn regarding selling property of another to the owner, concluding that the restriction is unnecessary and that the narration in al-Badā’i‘ may be outside Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah.

Key Terms
مرتهن (murtahin): The mortgagee (creditor) who holds collateral (rahn) as security for a debt.
مستأجر (musta’jir): The lessee, who has a right to usufruct (manfa‘a) of the leased property.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract; here, the act of nullifying a sale made without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relied-upon (muftā bihi) ruling regarding the mortgagee's right to rescind a sale made without his permission?
A) The mortgagee definitely has the right to rescind.
B) The mortgagee definitely does not have the right to rescind.
C) There is ikhtilāf among scholars; some fatwa authorities hold that he has the right, others that he does not.
D) The mortgagee can only ratify, not rescind.

Key Takeaway
The mortgagee's right in the corpus may entitle him to rescind a sale made without permission, unlike the lessee whose right is limited to usufruct.

Conflicting Dispositions by a Fuduli: The Hierarchy of Contracts
Exposition of rulings when multiple fuduli contracts conflict and which prevails upon ratification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hierarchy of conflicting fuduli contracts and determine which contract prevails upon simultaneous ratification.

Core Idea
Emancipation, kitabah, and tadbir override all; sale over lease and mortgage; gift with possession equals sale.

When a fuduli (unauthorized agent) makes multiple conflicting dispositions on the same property, and the owner subsequently ratifies them all simultaneously, not all contracts can stand. The Hanafi school provides a clear hierarchy to resolve such conflicts, giving priority to binding dispositions (itq, kitabah, tadbir) over non-binding ones, and among non-binding ones, sale takes precedence over lease, mortgage, and marriage. This lesson unpacks the rules and exceptions from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting dispositions
Look at the types of contracts made by the fuduli: sale, lease, mortgage, marriage, gift, emancipation, kitabah, tadbir. Determine if they are binding (lazim) or non-binding.
Binding dispositions include emancipation, kitabah, tadbir; others are non-binding.
2
Apply the hierarchy for binding vs non-binding
Binding contracts always prevail over non-binding ones. So if an emancipation and a sale conflict, the emancipation wins.
3
Among non-binding, determine relative strength
Sale is stronger than lease and mortgage. Lease is stronger than mortgage. Gift with possession equals sale. Marriage is the weakest; if two marriages conflict, both are void.
Sale is stronger because it transfers full ownership; lease only gives usufruct.
4
Handle two sales or two gifts
If two fuduli sell the same item to different buyers and both are ratified, the item is divided equally, and each buyer may choose to take half or leave. Similarly for two gifts, they share equally.
Division applies only if the item is divisible; for indivisible items, see the text.
5
Check exceptions: ghasib (usurper) cases
If two usurpers exchange goods belonging to the same owner, ratification is invalid because the owner already owns both items. If they take from two owners, ratification is valid. For currency exchange, it's valid because currencies are not specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو عرضاً وصح عتق مشتر من غاصب بإجازة بيعه لا بيعه ولو قطعت يده عند المشتري فأجيز باعها فضولي من رجل وزوجها منه آخر فأجيزا معاً يثبت الأقوى فتصير مملوكة لا زوجة . ولو زوجاها كل من رجل فأجيز بطلاً، ولو باعها كل من رجل فأجيزا تتنصف بينهما ويخير كل منهما بين أخذ النصف أو الترك. ولو باعه فضولي وأجره آخر أو رهنه أو زوجه فأجيزا معاً ثبت الأقوى فيجوز البيع ويبطل غيره لأن البيع أقوى، وكذا تثبت الهبة إذا وهبه فضولي وآجره آخر. وكل من العتق والكتابة والتدبير أحق من غيرها لأنها لازمة بخلاف غيرها، والإجارة أحق من الرهن لإفادتها ملك المنفعة بخلاف الرهن والبيع أحق من الهبة لأن الهبة تبطل بالشيوع ففيما لا تبطل بالشيوع كهبة فضولي عبداً وبيع آخر إياه يستويان لأن الهبة مع القبض تساوي البيع في إفادة الملك وهبة المشاع فيما لا يقسم صحيحة فيأخذ كل النصف، ولو تبايع غاصباً عرضي لرجل واحد فأجاز المالك لم يجز لأن فائدة البيع بثبوت الملك في الرقبة والتصرف وهما حاصلان للمالك في البدلين بدون هذا العقد فلم ينعقد فلم يلحقه إجازة. ولو غصبا من رجلين وتبايعا وأجاز المالك جاز. ولو غصبا النقدين من واحد وعقدا الصرف وتقابضا ثم أجاز جاز لأن النقود لا تتعين في المعاوضات، وعلى كل واحد من الغاصبين مثل ما غصب؛ كذا في فتح القدير من آخر الباب وأما وصية الفضولي كما إذا أوصى بألف مال غيره أو بعين من ماله فأجاز المالك فهو مخير إن شاء سلمها وإن شاء لم من يسلم كالهبة؛ كذا في القنية من الوصايا. وبه علم حكم هبة الفضولي وسيأتي في الصلح بيان صلح الفضولي والظاهر من فروعهم أن كل ما صح التوكيل به فإنه إذا باشره الفضولي يتوقف إلا الشراء بشرطه السابق .
If an item appears and the emancipation of a purchaser from a usurper is valid by ratification of his sale, not by the sale itself. And if his hand were cut off while with the purchaser and he ratified: a fuduli sold her to a man and another married her to him, and both are ratified simultaneously, the stronger is established, so she becomes a slave, not a wife. And if each of two men married her, and ratification is given, it is void. And if each of two men sold her, and both are ratified, she is divided half between them, and each is given choice between taking half or leaving. And if a fuduli sold him and another leased him or mortgaged him or married him, and both are ratified simultaneously, the stronger is established, so the sale is valid and the others are void because sale is stronger. Similarly, the gift is established if a fuduli gifted him and another leased him. And each of emancipation, kitabah, and tadbir is more entitled than others because they are binding unlike others. And lease is more entitled than mortgage because it gives ownership of usufruct unlike mortgage. And sale is more entitled than gift because gift is invalidated by joint ownership; so in what is not invalidated by joint ownership, like a fuduli gifting a slave and another selling him, they are equal because gift with possession equals sale in giving ownership, and gift of joint property in what cannot be divided is valid, so each takes half. And if two usurpers exchanged items with one person and the owner ratified, it is not valid because the benefit of sale is establishing ownership of the corpus and disposition, and both are already obtained by the owner in the two counter-values without this contract, so it did not take effect, so ratification does not attach to it. And if they usurped from two persons and exchanged, and the owner ratified, it is valid. And if they usurped the two currencies from one person and contracted a currency exchange and took possession, then ratified, it is valid because currencies are not specified in transactions, and each usurper is liable for the like of what he usurped. Thus in Fath al-Qadir from the end of the chapter. And as for the bequest of a fuduli, such as when he bequeaths a thousand from someone else's property or a specific item from his property, and the owner ratifies, he has the choice: if he wishes he hands it over, and if he wishes he does not, like a gift. Thus in al-Qunyah from the chapter of bequests. And by this the ruling of the gift of a fuduli is known, and the explanation of the fuduli's compromise will come in the chapter of compromise. And it appears from their branches that everything that can be delegated by agency, if a fuduli performs it, it becomes suspended, except purchase with its prior condition.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a statement of rulings on conflicting dispositions made by a fuduli (unauthorized agent) and the effects of subsequent ratification by the owner. The author presents a hierarchy: when two or more fuduli contracts conflict and the owner ratifies them simultaneously, the stronger contract prevails. For example, if one fuduli sells a slave and another marries the same slave to someone, and both are ratified, the sale prevails and the marriage is void—the slave becomes owned, not a wife. If two fuduli each marry the slave to different men and both marriages are ratified, both marriages are void. If two fuduli each sell the slave to different buyers and both sales are ratified, the slave is divided equally between the two buyers, and each buyer has the choice to take his half or leave it. When contracts are of different types, a clear hierarchy is established: emancipation (itq), kitabah (contract of manumission), and tadbir (postponed emancipation) are stronger than all others because they are binding (lazim). Lease (ijarah) is stronger than mortgage (rahn) because it grants ownership of usufruct. Sale is stronger than gift (hibah) because a gift is invalidated by joint ownership (shuyu'). However, in cases where the gift is not invalidated by joint ownership—such as gifting a single indivisible slave—gift with possession is equal to sale. The passage then discusses cases involving usurpers (ghasib): if two usurpers exchange items belonging to one owner and the owner ratifies, the exchange is not valid because the owner already owns both counter-values; no new ownership is achieved. But if the usurpers took from two different owners and exchanged, ratification makes it valid. If they usurped currency from one person and exchanged it with delivery, ratification makes it valid because currencies are not specific. Finally, the passage addresses bequest (wasiyyah) by a fuduli: if he bequeaths someone else's property and the owner ratifies, the owner has the choice to honor it or not, like a gift. The general rule is that any act that can be done by an agent, if a fuduli performs it, becomes suspended pending ratification, except purchase from someone with prior condition.

Key Terms
فضولي (fuduli): A person who acts without authorization or ownership, e.g., sells property that does not belong to him.
إجازة (ijazah): Ratification or approval by the owner after the fact, making a suspended contract effective.
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave, which is a binding disposition and prevails over non-binding ones.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a fuduli sells a slave and another fuduli leases the same slave to the same person, and the owner ratifies both simultaneously, which contract prevails?
A) The lease prevails because it grants usufruct.
B) The sale prevails because it is stronger.
C) Both are equally valid.
D) Both are invalid.

Key Takeaway
Among conflicting fuduli dispositions ratified simultaneously, the stronger contract prevails: binding dispositions (itq, kitabah, tadbir) are strongest, followed by sale, then lease, mortgage, and marriage is void if double.

Right of Lessee and Mortgagee to Rescind Fuduli Sale
Exposition of the lessee and mortgagee's right to rescind a fuduli sale with ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a lessee or mortgagee can rescind a fuduli sale, identify the differences between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the buyer's option, and apply the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Lessee cannot rescind; mortgagee's right is disputed, with the stronger view denying it.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on a statement by al-Karabisi distinguishing between the lessee and mortgagee regarding their ability to rescind a sale executed by a fuduli (unauthorized person). The commentator clarifies that the lessee has no right of rescission by consensus, while the mortgagee's position is subject to ikhtilaf among the Hanafi masters. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly applying the law of suspended sales and protecting parties' rights in lease and mortgage contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
The passage discusses two parties: the lessee (musta'jir) and the mortgagee (murtahin), and their reaction to a fuduli sale that affects their rights. Determine what each party stands to lose if the sale is executed without their permission.
The lessee's right is a right of detention (habs), while the mortgagee's right is a security interest.
2
State the general ruling for the lessee
The commentator states clearly that 'the lessee has no right to rescind the sale without disagreement'. This consensus applies regardless of the lessee's knowledge of the sale.
This is in contrast to the buyer's position, which depends on his knowledge at the time of purchase.
3
Examine the ikhtilaf regarding the mortgagee
While the mortgagor (the owner) also cannot rescind, there is a difference of opinion among the shaykhs about the mortgagee. The passage cites various sources indicating the stronger view is that the mortgagee cannot rescind.
The dispute is within the Hanafi school itself.
4
Analyze the buyer's option based on knowledge
The passage contrasts the positions of Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on whether a buyer who was aware of an existing lease or mortgage at the time of purchase has the option of rescission. Muhammad says yes; Abu Yusuf says no. The apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) supports Muhammad's view.
This issue is separate from the lessee/mortgagee's right but related.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequences
The relied-upon opinion for fatwa is that the mortgagee does not have the right to rescind. If the lessee does not ratify until the lease expires, the sale becomes effective. If the lessee ratifies, his right of detention is protected unless delivery also occurs.
The passage ends with a grammatical clarification and a critical remark about a phrase in the matn.

He said: (And the difference between them according to al-Karabisi (etc.) He affirmed it in al-Khaniyah in the chapter of suspended sale. And in al-Fath: The lessee has no right to rescind the sale without disagreement, nor does the mortgagor or the letter, and regarding the mortgagee there is difference of opinion among the shaykhs. And it was mentioned before that the buyer has the option of rescission if he did not know at the time of the sale about the lease or mortgage, and if he knew, it is the same according to Muhammad—it is said this is the apparent narration, and according to Abu Yusuf, no—and it is said this is the apparent narration. And in the Tashih of Shaykh Qasim, the shaykhs adopted this narration. But it is mentioned in Jami' al-Fusulayn that the first is the saying of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad and that it is the apparent narration. And in the Hashiyah of al-Ramli from al-Ghazzi that it is the correct view and upon it is the fatwa as in al-Walwalijiyyah. And al-Ramli transmitted from Munyat al-Mufti that it is the most correct. And in it from al-Zayla'i that the mortgagee does not have rescission according to the more correct of the two narrations. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn from al-Khaniyah: If the lessee does not ratify until the lease expires, the previous sale becomes effective. Similarly for the mortgagee if he pays off his debt. And in it from al-Dhakhira: The sale without the permission of the lessee becomes effective with respect to the seller and buyer, but not with respect to the lessee. So if the right of the lessee falls, that sale operates and there is no need for renewal, and this is the correct view. And if the lessee ratifies it, it becomes effective with respect to all, and he is not removed from possession to get his money, because his consent to the sale is considered for canceling the lease, not for removal from his possession. And from some of our shaykhs: that if he sold and delivered, and the lessee ratified both, his right of detention is invalidated; and if he ratified the sale but not the delivery, his right of detention is not invalidated. His statement: (the second) is the object of 'he ratified' and it is an adjective of an omitted thing, meaning he ratified the second sale. His statement: (and if the author had said 'he sold the property of another for its owner' it would be better) i.e., for the sake of its owner. Al-Ramli said: None of the shaykhs of the madhhab who composed the matns mentioned this restriction, and I say: leaving it is necessary, as indicated by the suspension of the usurper's sale as they explicitly stated without restriction, and as they stated regarding istihqaq (claim to ownership) that the claim to the sold item necessitates suspension of the contract on ratification, not its nullification in the apparent narration. And it appears that what he said in al-Bada'i is a narration outside the apparent narration, so reflect and refer to the branches mentioned in the two places mentioned, it will become clear.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's statement 'وفرق بينهما الكرابيسي' (and the difference between them according to al-Karabisi). The commentator clarifies the ruling regarding the right of the lessee and mortgagee to rescind a fuduli sale that affects their rights. The general rule: the lessee has no right to rescind the sale; there is consensus on this. The mortgagor also has no such right. For the mortgagee, there is a difference of opinion among the shaykhs. Some reports indicate that the buyer has an option (khiyar) if he was unaware of the lease or mortgage at the time of purchase. According to Imam Muhammad, this option exists even if he knew; this is said to be the apparent narration. According to Abu Yusuf, if he knew, he has no option. The text then presents the tarjih: the relied-upon opinion (mufta bihi) is that the mortgagee does not have the right to rescind. It cites various sources like al-Khaniyah, al-Fath, Jami' al-Fusulayn, and the Hashiyah of al-Ramli. The passage further discusses scenarios: if the lessee does not ratify until the lease expires, the previous sale becomes effective. Similarly for the mortgagee after paying his debt. If the lessee ratifies, the sale becomes effective for all parties, but the lessee is not evicted from possession – his consent is for canceling the lease, not for removal. There is also a detail about the right of detention: if the lessee ratifies both the sale and delivery, his lien right is invalidated; if he only ratifies the sale, his lien remains. Then the commentator explains grammatical points: 'الثاني' is the object of 'أجاز', meaning he ratified the second sale. Finally, a critical remark: the author of the matn suggests that the phrase 'باع ملك غيره لمالكه' (sold the property of another for its owner) would be better, but the commentator argues that this restriction is unnecessary because the ruling of suspension is clear without it, citing the example of the usurper's sale and the rules of istihqaq. The view of al-Bada'i on this matter is considered a rare narration outside the apparent narration.

Key Terms
مستأجر (musta'jir): The lessee, the person who has taken a property on lease, who has a right of detention until his dues are paid.
مرتهن (murtahin): The mortgagee, the person who holds a mortgage as security for a debt.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or cancellation of a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon opinion mentioned in the passage, does the mortgagee (al-murtahin) have the right to rescind a fuduli sale executed without his permission?
A) Yes, he has the right.
B) No, he does not have the right.
C) It depends on whether he knew about the mortgage at the time of sale.
D) Only if the sale was for a price less than market value.

Key Takeaway
The lessee has no right to rescind a fuduli sale; the mortgagee's right is disputed, and the relied-upon position is that he cannot rescind.

Emancipation by a Purchaser from a Usurper: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on emancipation after ratification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement between the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad regarding the validity of emancipation by a purchaser from a usurper, and state the proof each side uses.

Core Idea
Emancipation requires complete ownership; the dispute hinges on whether ratification grants complete ownership retrospectively.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a disagreement within the Hanafi school about a specific scenario: a person buys a slave from a usurper, and after the owner ratifies the sale, the buyer emancipates the slave. Is the emancipation valid? The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) say yes; Imam Muhammad says no. The debate centers on the nature of ownership conferred by a suspended contract and the interpretation of the hadith 'لا عتق لابن آدم فيما لا يملك'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fact pattern
State the sequence: usurper (ghasib) sells a slave to a buyer (mushtari). The original owner (malik) then ratifies the sale. The buyer then emancipates the slave. Determine the ruling on the emancipation.
2
State the ruling of the two shaykhs
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the emancipation is valid upon ratification. Their basis: the contract of the fuduli becomes effective retroactively upon ratification, thus the buyer owned the slave at the time of emancipation effectively.
Their view is the madhhab (zahir al-riwayah).
3
State Imam Muhammad's ruling and his proof
Muhammad holds that the emancipation is invalid because the buyer did not have complete ownership at the time of emancipation. He cites the hadith: 'لا عتق لابن آدم فيما لا يملك' (No emancipation for the son of Adam for what he does not own). He argues that the suspended contract does not confer complete ownership; even after ratification, ownership is retrospective only in some aspects, not fully.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala).
4
Analyze the analogies supporting Muhammad
The passage gives several analogies: (a) if the usurper himself emancipates and then pays compensation, his emancipation is invalid, even though his ownership via compensation is stronger than the buyer's. (b) If the buyer emancipates while the seller has an option (khiyar), it is invalid. (c) If the buyer sells the slave after possession and then the owner ratifies the first sale, the second sale is invalid, even though sale is quicker in effect than emancipation. (d) A mukatab and ma'dhun: their sale is valid but their emancipation is not.
These analogies show that incomplete ownership does not suffice for emancipation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: According to the two shaykhs, once the owner ratifies, the emancipation is valid and the slave is free. According to Muhammad, the emancipation is void; the slave remains a slave and the buyer may be liable for damages.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the view of the two shaykhs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وصح عتق مشتر من غاصب بإجازة بيعه لا بيعه وهذا عندهما. وقال محمد: لا يجوز عتقه أيضاً لأنه لم يملكه. وفي الحديث لا عتق لابن آدم فيما لا يملك»(۱) وهذا لأن عقد الفضولي موقوف وهو لا يفيده لعدم النفاذ وثبوته عند الإجازة استناداً فهو ثابت من وجه زائل من وجه فلا يصلح شرطاً للإعتاق وهو الملك الكامل لإطلاقه في الحديث وهو للكامل، ولذا لو أعتقه الغاصب ثم أدى الضمان لم يصح العتق مع أن الملك الثابت له بالضمان أقوى الملك الثابت للمشتري حتى ينفذ بيع الغاصب بأداء الضمان ولا ينفذ بيع من المشتري بإجازة المالك الأول ، وكذا لو أعتقه المشتري والخيار للبائع ثم أجاز البيع لا ينفذ عتقه، وكذا إذا قبض المشتري من الغاصب ثم باعه ثم أجاز المالك البيع الأول لم ينفذ البيع الثاني مع أن البيع أسرع نفاذاً من العتق حتى صح بيع المكاتب والمأذون دون عتقهما، ولذا لو باع الغاصب المغصوب ثم أدى الضمان نفذ بيعه ولو أعتقه ثم أدى الضمان لم ينفذ، وكذا المستحق مع لك ما قلناه فتدبر.
He said: (And the emancipation of a purchaser from a usurper is valid by ratification of his sale, not by the sale itself, and this is according to the two shaykhs. And Muhammad said: His emancipation is also not valid because he did not own him. And in the hadith: 'There is no emancipation for the son of Adam for that which he does not own.' This is because the contract of the fuduli is suspended and it does not give him ownership due to lack of effectiveness, and its establishment upon ratification is retrospective, so it is established from one aspect and non-existent from another, so it does not qualify as a condition for emancipation, which is complete ownership, because the hadith is absolute and refers to complete ownership. Thus, if the usurper emancipated him and then paid the guarantee, the emancipation is not valid, even though the ownership established for him by guarantee is stronger than the ownership established for the purchaser, to the extent that the sale of the usurper becomes effective by paying the guarantee, while the sale of the purchaser does not become effective by the ratification of the first owner. Similarly, if the purchaser emancipated him while the seller had an option, and then the sale was ratified, his emancipation does not take effect. Similarly, if the purchaser took possession from the usurper and then sold him, and then the owner ratified the first sale, the second sale does not take effect, even though sale is quicker in effectiveness than emancipation, to the point that the sale of a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) or a ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade) is valid, but their emancipation is not. Thus, if the usurper sold the usurped item and then paid the guarantee, his sale is effective, but if he emancipated him and then paid the guarantee, it is not effective. Similarly for the rightful owner—everything we have said, so reflect.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the emancipation of a slave by a purchaser who bought the slave from a usurper (ghasib). The matn states that the emancipation is valid after ratification of the sale (not the sale itself), and this is the position of the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Imam Muhammad disagrees, arguing that the emancipation is invalid because the purchaser never owned the slave completely. The foundation of the argument is the hadith: 'There is no emancipation for the son of Adam for that which he does not own.' Muhammad argues that the suspended contract of the fuduli does not confer ownership because it lacks effectiveness; when the owner ratifies, ownership attaches retrospectively, but it is a partial ownership from one aspect. Since complete ownership is necessary for emancipation, it is not valid. The commentator then provides analogical support: If the usurper himself emancipates the slave and later pays compensation (damān), the emancipation is not valid, even though his ownership via compensation is stronger than the purchaser's ownership via ratification. This is shown by the fact that the usurper's own sale becomes effective upon paying compensation, but the purchaser's sale does not become effective by the owner's ratification if there is a prior sale. Further analogies: if a purchaser emancipates a slave while the seller still has an option (khiyar), the emancipation is ineffective; if the purchaser, after taking possession, sells the slave and then the first sale is ratified, the second sale is ineffective, even though sale is quicker in effect than emancipation. The commentator also contrasts with the case of a mukatab (slave under contract) and a ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade): their sale is valid but their emancipation (by them) is not. Finally, the passage notes that if a usurper sells the usurped item and then pays compensation, the sale is effective; but if he emancipates and then pays compensation, it is not effective. The same principle applies to the rightful owner (mustahiqq). The student is urged to reflect carefully.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave, freeing him from servitude.
غاصب (ghasib): A usurper, one who takes property without right.
مشتر (mushtari): The buyer or purchaser, here specifically one who buys from a usurper.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee or compensation paid by the usurper for the usurped item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, why is the emancipation of a slave by a purchaser from a usurper invalid even after the owner ratifies the sale?
A) Because the purchaser bought the slave at an unfair price.
B) Because the purchaser did not have complete ownership at the time of emancipation.
C) Because the owner's ratification comes too late.
D) Because the slave is not present to be emancipated.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's relied-upon position is that emancipation by a purchaser from a usurper is valid after the owner ratifies the sale, but Imam Muhammad's opposing view based on the hadith remains a significant intra-school difference.

Refining al-Bada'i's View on Selling to Oneself
Critical refinement of a previous commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the correct interpretation of al-Bada'i's phrase 'sold it for himself' and distinguish it from a fuduli sale.

Core Idea
Al-Bada'i's 'sold it for himself' means selling to oneself, not as an agent.

In this passage, the commentator of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq responds to a critique of al-Bada'i's statement about sale by the owner himself. The critique claimed that al-Bada'i's view contradicts the madhhab's rules on fuduli sales. However, the commentator shows that the objection arises from a linguistic misunderstanding. The preposition 'li' in al-Bada'i's phrase is not causal but directional, meaning 'from'. Thus, the statement refers to an impossible contract where the same person is both seller and buyer, which is inherently void.

Guided Path
1
Identify the critique against al-Bada'i
The teacher cited al-Bada'i as saying that if a sold item is claimed (istihqaq), the contract is not rescinded in the apparent narration. The critic argued this contradicts the rule that a fuduli (unauthorized) sale is suspended. Understand the apparent problem.
The critic assumed 'sold for himself' means acting for his own benefit as a fuduli.
2
Analyze the linguistic ambiguity
The preposition 'li' (لام) in Arabic can mean 'for', 'to', or 'from'. The commentator argues that in al-Bada'i's phrase, 'li' means 'from', i.e., 'sold from himself (to himself)'. This changes the meaning entirely.
Compare: بعت له (I sold to him) vs. بعت منه (I sold from him).
3
Determine the correct interpretation
Al-Bada'i meant selling one's own property to oneself, which is impossible because one cannot be both seller and buyer. Therefore, the contract never takes effect (las yan'aqid). This is not a fuduli sale; it's a void contract from the start.
The critic's objection is based on a misreading.
4
Reconcile with the madhhab
The commentator concludes that al-Bada'i's statement does not contradict the madhhab's branches. The rule that fuduli sales are suspended remains intact. The apparent contradiction is resolved by proper linguistic understanding.
This shows the importance of precision in legal texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم رأيت في شرح تنوير الأبصار لمصنفه أقول : يشكل على هذا أي على ما نقله شيخنا عن البدائع ما قالوه من أن المبيع إذا استحق لا ينفسخ العقد في ظاهر الرواية بقضاء القاضي بالاستحقاق وللمستحق إجازته وجه الإشكال أن البائع باع لنفسه لا للمالك الذي هو أنه توقف على الإجازة ويشكل عليه الغاصب فإنه يتوقف على الإجازة فالظاهر ضعف ما في بيع البدائع، فلا ينبغي أن يعول عليه لمخالفته لفروع المذهب اهـ وهو عين ما قلناه ثم قال في شرح قوله ووقف بيع الغاصب لكن ظاهر إطلاق المشايخ التوقف على الإجازة يشكل ما قاله إلا أن يحمل على ما ذكرنا اهـ. والذي ذكره الحمل على أنه باعه لمالكه، ولا يخفى ما في هذا الحمل من البعد جداً فليتأمل اهـ . قلت : ويظهر لي أن ما في البدائع لا إشكال وإن ما فهمه المؤلف غير مراد البدائع، وذلك أن قول البدائع لو باعه لنفسه لم ينعقد أصلا معناه باعه من نفسه لأنه يلزم أن يكون بائعاً ومشترياً فاللام فيه بمعنى «من» فإنه قد يقال بعت له وبعت منه فاللام في عبارة البدائع ليست للتعليل حتى يكون احترازاً عما لو باعه لمالكه فكان على المؤلف أن يقول «ولو قال المصنف باع ملك ...
Then I saw in the Sharh Tanwir al-Absar by its author, I say: This is problematic, i.e., what our teacher transmitted from al-Bada'i that they said: if the sold item is claimed, the contract is not rescinded in the apparent narration by the judge's ruling of claim, and the claimant has the right to ratify it. The point of difficulty is that the seller sold for himself, not for the owner, so it is suspended on ratification. And the usurper is problematic because his sale is suspended on ratification. So it appears that what is in al-Bada'i regarding sale is weak, and should not be relied upon because it contradicts the branches of the madhhab. And that is exactly what we said. Then he said in the commentary on his statement: 'and the sale of the usurper is suspended', but the apparent unrestricted statement of the shaykhs that it is suspended on ratification contradicts what he said, unless it is interpreted as what we mentioned. And what he mentioned, the interpretation that he sold it for its owner, and it is not hidden that this interpretation is very far-fetched, so reflect. I say: It appears to me that what is in al-Bada'i is not problematic, and what the author understood is not what al-Bada'i meant. That is because al-Bada'i's statement 'if he sold it for himself, it does not take effect at all' means he sold it from himself (i.e., to himself), because he would be seller and buyer, so the preposition 'li' here means 'from' (min). For it is said 'I sold to him' and 'I sold from him.' The 'li' in al-Bada'i's phrase is not for causation such that it would be a restriction against selling for its owner. So the author should have said 'and if the author had said 'sold the property of...'
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) of the commentary. The commentator objects to a statement attributed to al-Bada'i that was cited by his teacher. According to the teacher's report, al-Bada'i claimed that when the sold item is claimed by a rightful owner (istihqaq), the contract is not rescinded in the apparent narration, and the claimant has the right to ratify it. The teacher found this problematic because if the seller sold for himself (not for the owner), the contract is suspended. And the usurper's sale is also suspended. So al-Bada'i's view seems weak. The commentator initially agrees with this objection. However, after further reflection, he offers a clarification: the phrase in al-Bada'i 'if he sold it for himself' (لو باعه لنفسه) actually means 'if he sold it from himself (to himself)', i.e., he sells his own property to himself, which is impossible because one person cannot be both seller and buyer. Therefore, the contract never takes effect at all, not because of suspension but because of impossibility. The preposition 'li' here is not for purpose (as in 'for the owner') but means 'from' (min). So this is not a counterexample to the rule that fuduli contracts are suspended. The commentator suggests that the author of the matn misunderstood al-Bada'i. The correct understanding is that al-Bada'i's statement is about a person selling his own property to himself, which is a void contract from the start. Therefore, the criticism is unfounded.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): The claim of rightful ownership by a third party that the sold item belongs to them.
بدائع (Bada'i): A reference to the book 'Bada'i' al-Sana'i' by al-Kasani, a major Hanafi fiqh work.
لام (lam): The preposition 'li' in Arabic, which can mean 'for', 'to', or 'from' depending on context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It means selling to himself, i.e., being both seller and buyer.
C) It means selling for himself as an agent.
D) It means selling without intention.

Key Takeaway
Al-Bada'i's statement 'sold it for himself' means selling to oneself, which is a void contract, not a fuduli sale, thus no contradiction with the madhhab.

Manumission by Buyer from Usurper: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Retrospective Ownership
Intra-Hanafi dispute on manumission with analogical comparisons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on manumission by a buyer from a usurper upon ratification, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and apply the underlying principle to analogous cases.

Core Idea
Manumission requires complete ownership at the time; retrospective ownership via ratification suffices for Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf but not for Muhammad.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the validity of manumission by a buyer who purchased a slave from a usurper. The owner's ratification of the sale (ijazah) is key. Within the Hanafi school, there is a disagreement: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the manumission valid, while Muhammad al-Shaybani considers it invalid. The dispute hinges on the interpretation of the hadith 'لا عتق لابن آدم فيما لا يملك' and the nature of ownership conferred by a suspended contract. The passage then tests this principle against several parallel cases to reveal the underlying legal criteria.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and the ikhtilaf
Identify the act: a buyer from a usurper manumits the slave. Then the owner ratifies the sale. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two), the manumission is valid. According to Muhammad, it is invalid. Note that the ruling for the sale itself is also affected: the sale is valid upon ratification, but manumission is valid only according to the two.
The text begins 'وهذا عندهما' indicating the view of the two is the relied-upon position.
2
Identify the proof (dalil) used by each party
Muhammad cites the hadith 'لا عتق لابن آدم فيما لا يملك' to argue that the buyer did not own the slave at the time of manumission. The two respond that ownership is established retrospectively upon ratification, thus satisfying the hadith's requirement of complete ownership. This is a classic example of interpreting 'complete ownership' (al-milk al-kamil) versus literal temporal ownership.
The hadith is transmitted by Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, and Ahmad.
3
Understand the concept of retrospective ownership (istinad) and its limitations
The contract of a fuduli is suspended (mawquf). Upon ratification, it becomes effective retrospectively, but the ownership is not fully complete in every aspect. The commentary explains that it is 'thabit min wajh, za'il min wajh' (established in one aspect, null in another). The two consider this sufficient for manumission; Muhammad does not.
This partial ownership is contrasted with ownership via guarantee (daman), which is stronger.
4
Compare with analogous cases
The author provides several comparisons to illustrate the principle: (a) If the usurper manumits and then pays damages, manumission is invalid, even though his ownership via guarantee is stronger. (b) If the buyer manumits while the seller had an option, and then the sale is ratified, manumission is invalid. (c) If the buyer resells the slave before ratification, the second sale is invalid, even though sale is more quickly effective than manumission. (d) A mukatib or ma'dhun can sell but not manumit, showing sale requires less stringent ownership.
These analogies reveal that manumission requires more complete ownership than sale.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) and the mufta bihi
According to the two, the slave is free upon ratification; according to Muhammad, the manumission is void and the slave remains owned. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the two, as indicated by the text's phrasing 'وهذا عندهما'.
In fatwa, the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وصح عتق مشتر من غاصب بإجازة بيعه لا بيعه وهذا عندهما. وقال محمد: لا يجوز عتقه أيضاً لأنه لم يملكه. وفي الحديث لا عتق لابن آدم فيما لا يملك) وهذا لأن عقد الفضولي موقوف وهو لا يفيده لعدم النفاذ وثبوته عند الإجازة استناداً فهو ثابت من وجه زائل من وجه فلا يصلح شرطاً للإعتاق وهو الملك الكامل لإطلاقه في الحديث وهو للكامل، ولذا لو أعتقه الغاصب ثم أدى الضمان لم يصح العتق مع أن الملك الثابت له بالضمان أقوى الملك الثابت للمشتري حتى ينفذ بيع الغاصب بأداء الضمان ولا ينفذ بيع من المشتري بإجازة المالك الأول، وكذا لو أعتقه المشتري والخيار للبائع ثم أجاز البيع لا ينفذ عتقه، وكذا إذا قبض المشتري من الغاصب ثم باعه ثم أجاز المالك البيع الأول لم ينفذ البيع الثاني مع أن البيع أسرع نفاذاً من العتق حتى صح بيع المكاتب والمأذون دون عتقهما، ولذا لو باع الغاصب المغصوب ثم أدى الضمان نفذ بيعه ولو أعتقه ثم أدى الضمان لم ينفذ، وكذا المستحق مع لك ما قلناه فتدبر.
His statement: (And it is valid the manumission of a buyer from a usurper by ratification of his sale, not his sale. And this is according to the two. And Muhammad said: His manumission is not permissible also because he did not own him. And in the hadith: 'There is no manumission for the son of Adam regarding what he does not own.') And this is because the contract of the fuduli is suspended and it does not benefit him due to lack of execution, and its establishment upon ratification is retrospective, so it is established in one aspect and null in another, so it is not suitable as a condition for manumission, which is complete ownership due to its absoluteness in the hadith, and it is for the complete. Hence, if the usurper manumitted him and then paid the guarantee, the manumission is not valid, even though the ownership established for him by guarantee is stronger than the ownership established for the buyer, to the extent that the sale of the usurper becomes effective by payment of guarantee, but the sale of the buyer does not become effective by ratification of the original owner. Similarly, if the buyer manumitted him while the seller had the option, and then he ratified the sale, his manumission does not take effect. Similarly, if the buyer took possession from the usurper and then sold him, and then the owner ratified the first sale, the second sale does not take effect, even though sale is more quickly effective than manumission, to the extent that the sale of a mukatib and a ma'dhun is valid but not their manumission. Hence, if the usurper sold the usurped property and then paid the guarantee, his sale becomes effective; but if he manumitted and then paid the guarantee, it does not become effective. And similarly for the claimant. Reflect on what we have said.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of manumission by a buyer from a usurper when the owner ratifies the sale. The text begins by stating the ruling that such manumission is valid according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), but Muhammad holds it is invalid because the buyer did not own the slave at the time of manumission. The proof cited is the hadith 'There is no manumission for the son of Adam regarding what he does not own.' The commentary explains that the contract of the fuduli is suspended; upon ratification it is established retrospectively but in an incomplete manner, thus it does not confer the complete ownership required for valid manumission. The author then draws comparisons with other cases: if the usurper himself manumits and then pays damages, the manumission is invalid even though his ownership through guarantee is stronger; if the buyer manumits when the seller had an option, the manumission does not take effect; if the buyer resells the property before ratification, the second sale does not take effect even though sale is more effective than manumission. These examples illustrate the principle that manumission requires complete ownership at the time of manumission, which is not achieved through a suspended contract.

Key Terms
فضولي (fuduli): A person who acts in a legal transaction without authorization, e.g., selling property that is not his own.
عتق (itq): Manumission of a slave, freeing him from servitude.
إجازة (ijazah): Ratification or approval of a contract by the rightful owner, making the contract effective.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; a contract that is not immediately effective but awaits ratification.
غاصب (ghasib): Usurper; one who wrongfully takes property without right.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee or compensation paid by the usurper to the owner for the usurped property, which can confer ownership.
خيار البائع (khiyar al-ba'i'): The option of the seller to rescind the sale; it prevents the transfer of full ownership until the option period ends.
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has a contract of manumission (kitabah) to earn freedom by paying installments.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who is permitted by his master to engage in trade; he can sell but not manumit.
استحقاق (istihqaq): Claim of ownership by a rightful owner, e.g., when a buyer's claim to a slave is invalidated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, when is the manumission of a buyer from a usurper valid?
A) Immediately upon the buyer's manumission, regardless of ratification.
B) After the owner ratifies the sale, because ownership is established retrospectively.
C) If the usurper pays damages, because that gives stronger ownership.
D) Only if the buyer pays damages to the owner.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that manumission by a buyer from a usurper is valid upon the owner's ratification, as retrospective ownership satisfies the requirement of complete ownership for manumission.

Refining the Ruling of Sale by a Fuduli for Himself: Interpreting 'Li Nafsihi' in al-Bada'i'
Refines the ruling in Bada'i' regarding sale by a fuduli for his own benefit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a fuduli selling for his own benefit and a fuduli selling to himself, and apply the correct ruling for each scenario.

Core Idea
A fuduli selling 'for himself' means selling to himself — a void act — not selling for his own gain, which is suspended.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses a common confusion regarding a statement in al-Bada'i' about a fuduli (unauthorised agent) selling property for himself. The author refutes an objection by clarifying that the phrase 'li nafsihi' means 'from himself' (i.e., to himself), not 'for his own benefit'. This linguistic insight resolves an apparent conflict with the well-established Hanafi ruling that a fuduli's sale is suspended upon ratification by the owner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting statements
Locate the statement from al-Bada'i' that a fuduli selling 'for himself' results in a contract that does not conclude, and the objection from Sharh Tanwir al-Absar that this conflicts with the rule that a fuduli's sale is suspended.
The objection claims that if the sold item is claimed by a third party, the contract is not dissolved and the claimant can ratify, implying the sale is valid only upon ratification.
2
Understand the objection
Analyze why the critic thinks Bada'i' is weak: the critic assumes Bada'i' is discussing a fuduli selling for his own benefit, which would normally be suspended. But Bada'i' says it does not conclude at all, which seems contradictory.
The critic is influenced by the typical case of a usurper where the sale is suspended.
3
Analyze the response: linguistic reinterpretation
The author of Bahr al-Raiq explains that the lam in 'li nafsihi' is a lam meaning 'from' (min), not a lam of causation. Thus, 'li nafsihi' means 'from himself' or 'to himself', i.e., the fuduli sells the item to himself, which is void because one cannot be both seller and buyer.
Arabic idiom: 'bictu lahu' (I sold to him) and 'bictu minhu' (I sold from him) show that lam can replace min.
4
Apply the correct ruling to each scenario
Distinguish: (1) Fuduli sells to himself → batil (void) because the same person cannot be both parties. (2) Fuduli sells to a third party for his own benefit → mawquf (suspended) pending owner's ratification. The statement in Bada'i' refers to the first scenario, not the second.
This resolves the apparent contradiction: Bada'i' is correct and consistent with other Hanafi rulings.
5
Evaluate the strength of the interpretation
Consider why the author's interpretation is preferable: it preserves the consistency of the madhhab and avoids condemning a well-respected source. The alternative (critic's) would require dismissing Bada'i' as weak, which is unnecessary.
The author explicitly says the critic's interpretation is far-fetched and urges reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then I saw in Sharh Tanwir al-Absar by its author, I say: This poses a problem for what our teacher transmitted from al-Bada'i', that if the sold item is claimed, the contract is not dissolved in the apparent riwayah by the judge's ruling of claim, and the claimant has the right to ratify it. The point of objection is that the seller sold for himself, not for the owner, meaning that it is suspended upon ratification. And the case of the usurper poses a problem because it is suspended upon ratification. So apparently, what is in Bada'i' is weak, and should not be relied upon because it contradicts the branches of the madhhab. That is exactly what we said. Then he said in explaining his statement 'and the sale of the usurper is suspended', but the apparent general application of the later scholars that it is suspended upon ratification poses a problem to what he said, unless it is interpreted as we mentioned. And what he mentioned is the interpretation that he sold it to its owner. And it is not hidden that this interpretation is very far-fetched, so reflect. I say: It appears to me that there is no problem in Bada'i', and what the author understood is not what Bada'i' meant. That is because the statement of Bada'i' 'if he sold it for himself, it does not conclude at all' means he sold it from himself, because it would necessitate him being both seller and buyer, so the lam here means 'from', as it is said 'I sold to him' and 'I sold from him'. So the lam in the phrase of Bada'i' is not for causation such that it would be a distinction from the case where he sold it to its owner. So the author should have said 'if the author said: He sold the property
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a statement in al-Bada'i' regarding the sale by a fuduli when he sells for himself (li nafsihi) versus for the owner (li malikihi). The author first presents an objection raised in Sharh Tanwir al-Absar: the statement in Bada'i' that if the sold item is claimed (mustahaq) the contract is not dissolved, and the claimant can ratify, conflicts with the principle that a fuduli selling for himself makes the contract suspended. The objector argues that in the case of a usurper, the sale is suspended, so Bada'i' is weak. Then the author responds by clarifying that the phrase 'for himself' in Bada'i' actually means 'from himself' (i.e., selling to himself), which would be void because a person cannot be both seller and buyer. The lam (li) here is not a lam of causation but a lam meaning 'from' as in Arabic idiom. Thus, Bada'i' is not about selling for one's own benefit but about selling to oneself. This resolves the apparent contradiction. The author emphasizes that the original author of the Sharh misunderstood Bada'i'.

Key Terms
بدائع (Badai'): A Hanafi fiqh work by al-Kasani (d. 587/1191).
استحقاق (istihqaq): Claim of ownership by a third party, e.g., when someone proves the sold item belongs to them.
لام في عبارة البدائع (lam fi 'ibarat al-Badai'): The preposition 'li' in the phrase 'bai'ahu li nafsihi' which the author interprets as meaning 'from' (i.e., min), not 'for'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) For his own benefit
B) To himself (from himself)
C) Without owner's permission
D) With fraudulent intent

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'li nafsihi' in Bada'i' means 'to himself', not 'for his own benefit'; the former is void, the latter is suspended.
